FA8075-18-D-0004

Project ADRIAN
An Analysis of Logistics Data Exchange
Requirements at the Tactical Edge
Abstract: This study is the culmination of a comprehensive examination of the data
prioritization and exchanges required to enable logistics support to tactical,
geographically disparate Marine forces operating in command and control (C2) denied or
degraded environments (C2D2E). This analysis identifies baseline logistics data
exchange capabilities required by Stand-in Forces (SIF) conducting Expeditionary
Advanced Base Operations (EABO) against near-peer adversaries across the
competition continuum.

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004

Abstract
This report is the culmination of a comprehensive examination of the data exchange
requirements and corresponding prioritization to enable logistics support to tactical,
geographically disparate Marine forces operating in command and control (C2) denied or
degraded environments (C2D2E). This analysis identifies baseline logistics request capabilities
required by Stand-in Forces (SIF) conducting Expeditionary Advanced Base Operations (EABO)
against a peer or near-peer adversary, and across the competition continuum.
Additionally, this analysis supports the risk assessment and decision-making processes for
logisticians to develop recommendations optimizing combat readiness and meet demands (e.g.,
survivability and lethality) in the context of specific threat capabilities against U.S. forces in the
USINDOPACOM area of operations (AO). The analysis recommends improvements to the SIF
through new technology, innovative solutions, doctrine, C2, warfare concepts, military
operations center procedures, future concepts, and has enterprise-wide application across the
range of military operations.
This initiative was conducted under the cognizance of Logistics Combat Element (LCE)
Integration Division (LCE ID), Capabilities Development Directorate (CDD), Deputy
Commandant for Combat Development and Integration (DC CD&I), in support of the Logistics
Information Technology (IT) Capabilities Based Assessment (CBA).

Preface
This report details the minimum data requirements from the perspective of demand initiation,
primarily from the smallest, most forward deployed tactical user in a communications
degraded/denied environment. This report is directed primarily to those in the position to map
and create the data architecture necessary to pass the minimum demand and request
information, either vertically or horizontally, in the fulfillment of the demand. This report applies
to those who are tasked with developing data exchanges across multiple types of networks and
communication nodes either via secure or non-secure means and across security enclaves.
This work represents many hours of research on what data is, what it means, where it comes
from, how data is used, how data is developed, doctrinal understanding, and candid
conversations between ground truth reality and perceived assumptions. The report is part of the
greater work to develop a single, holistic approach to five of the six doctrinal functions of Marine
Corps logistics and their applicable subfunctions as it stands today without depending on future
material capabilities yet to be matured and employed.1
While this report is voluminous—perhaps dauntingly so—it is necessary to clearly articulate the
environment, context, rationale, and urgency of a unified, holistic logistics data solution for the
survivability of our most valuable resource—the Marine. There exists a moral and an ethical
imperative to do this the right way, and to protect our people accordingly. Failure to address
these findings carries potential mortal consequences.
Lastly, this report identifies and discusses doctrinal and capability gaps discovered through the
various working groups and breakout sessions. What is unique about this report is the
discussion on the rationale, impacts, and then the granular recommendations which then will
enable future capabilities to be integrated into the Marine Corps Installations and Logistics
Enterprise (MCILE).

1 The sixth doctrinal function of Marine Corps Logistics—Services—is omitted from this report as it is not

applicable to tactical-level logistics operations in a warfighting environment.

1

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004

Section 1: Executive Summary
The Global War on Terror, in many ways, spurred a revolution in military technology and
accelerated an evolution in tactics, techniques, and procedures. In other areas, however, we as
a military have become complacent. With uncontested freedom of movement throughout the
battlespace, sustaining our combat forces was easy, and the competencies of our military
logistics apparatus severely atrophied. In Iraq and Afghanistan, we were primarily engaged in
counterinsurgency-type warfare against adversaries with comparatively primitive
communications systems. Self-assured of our technological superiority, our logistics information
systems experienced little advancement or improvement during those twenty years of conflict in
the Middle East.
However, the great power competition has seen the rise of new threats and a different class of
opponent entirely. Ongoing geopolitical tension with both China and Russia has signaled a
refocus on what conflict might look like with a near-peer adversary. We can no longer afford to
take for granted that our adversary’s most sophisticated electronics are cell phones and walkietalkies, or that our forces will not have to operate in an environment where our ability to
communicate may be degraded or denied entirely.
The Marine Corps’ logistics information technology (IT) portfolio currently has two major
weaknesses. First, there exist 77 unique, stand-alone software systems meant to enable
specific, tasks. There is effectively no interoperability or compatibility between any of these
disparate applications; data cannot be exchanged between them without manual transcription
from human operators. The inability to automate processes that require data input from different
sources not only takes up valuable man-hours that Marines could devote to other missioncritical tasks, it also greatly increases the odds of human error. Moreover, the time that it
currently takes for leaders to assemble and process this information lends itself to delays in
decision-making that could have catastrophic real-world implications. The second issue is even
more alarming. At present, every one of those 77 logistics applications—and nearly all logistics
data—reside on unclassified networks and servers. This poses an enormous risk to both
mission and force. Our adversaries possess significant cyber and electronic warfare capabilities.
Non-secure communications are especially susceptible to interception and exploitation. Our
unsecured logistics data feeds the enemy’s ‘kill chain’ and can be used to target our forces. The
very real, tangible consequence of continuing to operate under this paradigm is loss of life.
The Project ADRIAN initiative was born out of moral imperative to address these critical
vulnerabilities. Our mission is to deliver an IT-enabled logistics command and control (C2)
capability to the operating forces, one that ensures the integrity and availability of logistics data
and provides for its secure and reliable exchange between the warfighter and the relevant
combat support agency. That logistics C2 functionality must be holistically integrated into the
Common Operational Picture (COP). This study is just the first phase of Project ADRIAN—
define information exchange requirements. This detailed analysis deconstructs the functions of
Marine Corps logistics to identify core tasks and determine all the information and data that a
future-state IT system would need to accommodate. Additionally, the critical data analysis
contained herein identifies the minimum data exchanges required to enable logistics operations
and effectively sustain our forces in communications denied or degraded environments (C2D2).
Publication of this document signifies a transition to development and engineering of a software
solution that will strengthen and streamline our logistics processes and enhance our ability to
face new and emerging threats.
signed by

Section 2: Introduction
2.1 Purpose
This section provides the operational context in which logistics operations will occur. Logistics
information technology (IT) systems have a critical dependency on communications network
pathways. A logical assumption is the data pathways and information exchange requirements
need to be predicated on the operating conditions. If the system is designed around austere
conditions, enemy threat capabilities, and operational mission requirements, then rationally, it
should work well in an uncontested environment. Therefore, the purpose of this analysis is
driven by the operating environment, near-peer threat, the Stand-in Force (SIF) mission, and the
resulting logistical challenges these changes present.

2.1.1 Operating Environment
The United States (U.S.) Indo-Pacific Command (USINDOPACOM) area of responsibility (AOR)
provides the operational context from which this analysis is derived. The AOR encompasses
about half the earth's surface, stretching from the waters off the west coast of the U.S. to the
western border of India, and from Antarctica to the North Pole. Within the AOR boundaries, 83%
of the surface area is water, with only 17% of the surface comprised of land mass.
This region is unparalleled in its diversity—culturally, socially, economically, and geopolitically—and is home to more than 50% of the world's population. The AOR comprises 36
national identities, 3,000 unique languages, several of the world's largest militaries, five nations
allied with the U.S. through mutual defense treaties (Australia, Japan, the Republic of Korea
(ROK), the Philippines, and Thailand), and two nations opposed or confrontational to the U.S.
(People's Republic of China (PRC) and Democratic People's Republic of Korea (DPRK)). The
region encompasses over 25,000 inhabited and uninhabited islands in varying size and shape.

2.1.2 Near-Peer Threat
The following assessment provides the context within the great power competition against
potential near-peer adversaries (i.e., China) in contested operating environments. This presents
compounding challenges for freedom of movement and domain dominance that logistics
communication, coordination, and execution must consider.
2.1.2.1 Diplomatic Efforts
China is the preeminent near-peer competitor to the U.S. and is challenging free and open
shipping lanes by claiming sovereignty over maritime regions and territories. Diplomatically,
China has established formal relations with Malaysia, Thailand, the Philippines, Bangladesh,
Maldives and recently Fiji and Papua New Guinea to expand its influence to gain additional
footholds and bolster its maritime claims.
2.1.2.2 Technological Capabilities
As a near-peer threat, China possesses technological capabilities that are equivalent—or in
some cases superior—to those of the U.S. The following are some of the anti-access, area
denial (A2AD) capabilities representing a direct threat to U.S. forces across the range of military
operations in the USINDOPACOM AOR.
1) Satellite surveillance. According to a U.S. Department of Defense (DoD) report to
Congress in November 2022, China nearly doubled the number of intelligence,
surveillance, and reconnaissance (ISR) satellite platforms in the past four years, and its
total satellite fleet, numbering 260 platforms, is second only to that of the United States.

3

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
This provides China with the ability to employ a persistent and consistent observation
capability over the operating area. It is assumed these satellites are capable of visible,
imagery at a resolution of around 10 centimeters, coupled with additional multispectral,
hyperspectral, and synthetic aperture radar capabilities on par with U.S. systems. [1]
2) Electromagnetic (EM) detection and deception. Electromagnetic Spectrum Superiority is
firstly about people and is a twofold capability. On the detection side, the People’s
Liberation Army (PLA) is using drones, satellites, and cyber monitoring to find
electromagnetic “heat patterns” to confirm the presence of military equipment and
personnel. Every electronic and electro-mechanical device emits an electromagnetic
signal and is detectible within a given range. Additionally, deception targets human
decision-makers. Notionally, the PLA could target U.S. satellite uplinks and downlinks
supporting ISR, communications, early warning, and navigation systems. The
consequences could have a profound negative impact on a commander’s planning,
decision-making, and execution cycle, and complicate effective air, ground, and naval
operations. To achieve the above objectives, the PLA emphasizes the deception
strategy of “hide the real and inject the false,” effecting signals and information to
mislead U.S. operators and decision-makers. They also highlight the importance of
surprise — as the perceived weaker side in a competition, employing asymmetric means
to defeat the stronger rival via a fast electromagnetic spectrum attack against a critical
vulnerability when least expected. [2]
3) Cyber detection and service interruption. For years, China has tested its cyber
capabilities by targeting the personal data of Americans, including the theft of personnel
records from the U.S. Office of Personnel Management (OPM), the intrusion into Marriott
hotels, Anthem health insurance company, and now the wholesale theft of credit and
other information from Equifax. Additionally, China has practiced its ability to interrupt
and deny cyber services as a pressure tactic against its neighbors. [2]
4) Radio signal detection and jamming. Since 2020, Chinese military’s ground-based
jammers have seen a severalfold increase in numbers, potentially targeting the most
distant and powerful U.S. communications satellites. It is a development that poses an
expanded threat to a critical enabler of modern military warfare to include the global
positioning systems (GPS) utility that provides users with positioning, navigation, and
timing (PNT) services which almost every major piece of equipment uses. [1] [3]
5) Cellular monitoring and service interruption. China has and continues to exploit
vulnerabilities in the global telecommunications network to target, track and intercept
phone communications of U.S. phone subscribers traveling abroad as they connect to
foreign cell towers. In 2018, China carried out the highest number of apparent
surveillance actions targeting U.S. mobile phone users over 3G and 4G networks
through Chinese state-owned Unicom. Tens of thousands of phone users were affected
by the attacks between 2018 and 2020. This mass surveillance incident was intended
primarily for intelligence collection. Such attacks pinpoint the location of U.S. based cell
phones operating abroad which indicate a mass of U.S. forces. Additionally, such
methods that are used for monitoring can also be used for service denial. A simple
command can shut down all cellular service for U.S. based cellphones operating on a
foreign cellular tower. [4]
6) Financial monitoring and service interruption. China has proven its ability to intrude and
monitor financial transactions through data gateways. While the actual transaction data
may not be compromised, the header transaction data is readable allowing monitoring of
the country origin of the financial instrument (e.g., credit cards, debit cards, etc.) and the

4

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
location of the point of sale. Through this type of monitoring China can ascertain U.S.
financial activity within a particular foreign country exposing vendors and U.S. forces to
hostilities and coordinated cyber-attacks. [5]
While the technological capabilities are impressive, it is impossible for our near-peer competitor
to shut down all communications pathways indefinitely. The collective energy output and
coverage required to do so is impractical and unsustainable. Therefore, the more logical
approach is that these capabilities will be applied to targeted areas and with limited duration
against key U.S. forces and capabilities.
2.1.2.3 Military Strategy and Capabilities
China’s investments in ISR and its precision-strike complex have enabled operational reach to
the U.S. mainland. However, these capabilities are more likely applicable within the
USINDOPACOM region. For at least the past three decades, China has studied U.S. concepts
for joint force power projection and maneuver warfare. In response, China developed strategies
and capabilities to counter these concepts, aiming to keep the joint force at “arm’s length,”
disrupt U.S. planning, and complicate U.S. decision-making.
While China does not make its military capabilities transparent, it is known that it has expended
significant resources on research, development, and procurement of ship mounted, long range
missile technologies. The table below represents the current estimate of China’s shipborne
missile capabilities. [6]

Table 1: China's Shipborne Missile Capability

Based on the presented data, it is reasonable to assume the Chinese military strategy is one of
“detect, triangulate, and engage” from mobile platforms to provide significant stand-off from U.S.
engagement capabilities. China’s intent is to avoid direct engagement with large, land-based
forces, in favor of an asymmetric strategy.

2.1.3 The Stand-in Force Concept of Operations
To defeat the Chinese strategy of “detect, triangulate, and engage” at distance, the SIF mission
and strategy is adapted from large land-based maneuver warfare competencies (easily
detectable, easily engaged) to small, lethal, low signature, and highly mobile elements (not
easily detectable, not easily engaged) to deny the enemy maritime mobility and key terrain.
The main ideas behind the SIF concept are simple:
1) Find. Detect and/or discover a potential adversary’s people and things (such as weapon
systems, sensor systems, submarines, etc.) in an assigned operating area and then
track them at a level that facilitates targeting by fleet or joint weapons until they leave
that area (i.e., kill chain concept). This finding and tracking effort starts as soon as the

5

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
possible target is identified and continues at every point along the competition
continuum. [7]
2) Strike. If armed conflict begins, the SIF will use knowledge of the adversary to help the
fleet or other elements of the joint force attack quickly and effectively, blind the
adversary, and disrupt the enemy’s plans to force a move into other places where SIF
and the fleet have an advantage. [7]
3) Deny. In the event of armed conflict, SIF will conduct sea denial in specific areas,
disrupting adversarial plans. Sea denial is nested within the fleet and joint force
campaign plans. For example, denying a specific area can present a forward edge
behind which the U.S. fleet can maneuver. Area denial can also “herd” an adversary into
areas in which U.S. forces have a relative advantage, or it can sanitize key straits or
chokepoints. [7]
4) Counter. The forward SIF element must make itself difficult to detect by a potential
adversary by maintaining a low signature, moving frequently and unpredictably, and
using deception. This imposes costs on potential adversaries, as they are forced to
expend resources to find and engage friendly forces. Winning the counterreconnaissance fight in the context of a naval campaign also means that Marines in the
littorals will screen for the fleet as a measure of force protection, and thereby increase
the fleet commander’s freedom of action/movement/decision options. This enables
fighting inside a contested area and disrupts an adversary’s plans. [7]

2.1.4 The Logistics Challenge
While the SIF operating construct is relatively simple in design, maintaining and sustaining
these elements operating in geographically disparate locations across the spectrum of
competition introduces complex challenges for logistical support. Logistically speaking, this is a
new challenge to plan, communicate, and execute. Adding to the challenge, the SIF may
include elements from the Army, Marine Corps, Navy, Coast Guard, Air Force, Space Force,
special operations forces (SOF), interagency organizations, as well as allies and partners.
These factors introduce a completely new dimension not previously exercised as a core
competency. Therefore, logistics—as a whole—is then challenged to collect the right data at the
appropriate level, and to exchange that data between the relevant agencies to enable effective
decision-making by commanders at both supporting and supported units. [7]

2.1.5 Purpose Summary
The driving force behind this analysis is the collective logistics challenges presented by the
operating environment, the SIF and EABO operating concepts, and the threat posed by nearpeer adversaries across the competition continuum. When accounting for these factors, a new
imperative emerges to determine the minimum data exchange requirements. Therefore, the
focus is only on the most critical data elements that must be transmitted.

2.2 Objective
2.2.1 Marine Corps Enterprise Objectives
In response to the factors of the operating environment, enemy threat, and new operating
missions, logistics data must be prioritized the same as — and fall under the umbrella of —
what is considered “kill chain” traffic. To transform and prioritize logistics data as kill chain traffic,
the Marine Corps’ objectives include, but are not limited to:

6

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
1) Define the current logistics operating environment (as-is state).
2) Outline the capabilities and systems that enable the current state.
3) Identify gaps and overlaps.
4) Define requirements at the tactical level (to-be state).
5) Outline future capabilities to achieve the to-be state.
6) Reconcile gaps and overlaps between the as-is and to-be state.
7) Generate a capabilities requirement document—or series thereof—to address gaps and
operational risks identified in the Log IT CBA, and present recommendations for material
and procedural solutions.
These objectives enable planning and execution of logistics operations to nest with and support
the SIF. Logistics is the most data intensive warfighting function, dependent upon multiple
sources and streams at various levels to enable critical decision making and execution.
Just as maneuver warfare concepts require force redesign and optimization to counter the
enemy threat, logistics too must parse the data inputs and streams down to the most critical
elements to initiate action and register demand.

2.2.2 Analysis Objective
The objective of this analysis is to support the overall Marine Corps’ objectives. However, not all
of the previous objectives will be met in this analysis, but they will support parallel and ongoing
analyses and initiatives headed by other elements of the enterprise. As such, this analysis is to
apply scientific based methods to identify the minimum data exchange requirements at the
lowest tactical level. Identifying the critical data inputs from the tactical level flowing upward to
initiate logistics decision/action is the foundation upon which to build out further logistics
capabilities.

2.3 Scope
The scope of this analysis is limited to ground logistics at the lowest tactical level
communicating upward and the minimal essential data elements for five out of six functions of
logistics and their corresponding sub-functions. The omitted doctrinal function of Marine Corps
logistics is ‘Services’, which is mainly an internal command function with minimal external
support requirements.
Aviation logistics is beyond the scope of this analysis. However, the findings contained herein
may be relevant in applying a similar methodology to identify the minimum data exchange
requirements and pathways in these related fields.
Additionally, this study provides a comparative analysis incorporating modern industry
standards/concepts and then operationalizing them to fit into the current military data exchange
requirements where appropriate and logical. While focusing on the threat environment, it strives
to present a holistic approach in all aspects of logistics information management regardless of
operating conditions.
This analysis supports Contract FA8075-18-D-0004, Task 3.4.7. in analyzing the risk
assessment and decision analysis for logistics to develop recommendations optimizing combat
readiness and meet demands (e.g., survivability and lethality) in the context of specific threat
capabilities against III MEF forces in the first island chain. The analysis recommends
improvements to the Stand-in Force through new technology, innovative solutions, C2, warfare

7

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
concepts, military operation center processes, and future concepts and has enterprise-wide
application.

2.4 Methodology, Assumptions, and Procedures
2.4.1 Problem Framing
The Logistics IT portfolio—as it stands today—is not capable of supporting Stand-in Forces
operating in austere, contested, communications denied/degraded, and distributed maritime
environments. The lack of a holistic logistics C2 capability prevents the secure and timely
exchange of data required to enable combat support to the tactical edge, thereby presenting an
enormous risk to mission and force. The following sections provide the analytical basis of the
problem frame assessment.
2.4.1.1 Quantity of Logistics Information Technology Systems
As of this analysis, there are currently 77 unique logistics IT systems in Marine Corps
inventory.2 That number averages to over 12 systems for each of the six doctrinal functions of
logistics. The sheer volume of logistics information systems operating on existing network
architectures is unsustainable and incapable of supporting warfighter needs at the tactical edge,
especially given the defined operating environment.
2.4.1.2 Operating Enclaves
All logistics information systems currently in inventory are needlessly complex, cumbersome
and resource intensive (data center storage, processing power, bandwidth, and throughput
requirements), and reside primarily and/or exclusively on the Non-Secure Internet Protocol
Router (NIPR) Network (NIPRNet). With these systems existing exclusively on NIPR, the ability
to exchange data between security enclaves is severely restricted—impractical in most cases, if
not outright impossible.3 Logistics data is routed through or to many joint force military and/or
interagency organizations, and may ultimately be routed through or to hundreds of potential
contracted vendors. Unsecure data traversing unsecure networks is susceptible to enemy
interception and exploitation and may ultimately feed the enemy’s kill chain. A simple resupply
request, for instance, can indicate composition, disposition, strength, and operational capability
of friendly forces at a given, precise location. Given the projected operating conditions in the
USINDOPACOM AOR, unclassified networks will be unavailable and there is a high likelihood of
limited or severely restricted access to the Secure Internet Protocol Router (SIPR) Network
(SIPRNet). Unsecured logistics data constitutes targeting information that feeds the enemy’s
“kill chain” and represents an enormous force protection risk.
2.4.1.3 Quality of Service
1) Gaps in Service. While there are approximately 12 information systems per function of
logistics, it is noted these systems do not cover the full logistical process requirement.
Out of 1,800 logistics processes described in doctrine, policy, and directives, 0%
originate digitally and are not holistically supported by information technology. 8% of
those processes have some support, while 92% have no support. Initiating, supporting,
and terminating records do not start digitally, remain digital, and/or are stored digitally
within the logistics information system(s). The preponderance of records are paper
based and stored in a file apart from the supporting system. Records that were found to
2 20 November 2022 USMC Logistics Systems Inventory
3 Out of 77 logistics information systems, only two are authorized on SIPR and CENTRIX: TCPT and

CLC2S but these two systems are not cross-domain solutions (feeding data across enclaves).

8

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
be digital were scanned from paper sources and did not originate as a digital record
organically within the logistics system. Paper and digital records are combined into a
self-competing system of filing and maintenance which do little to complement each
other. At no point could a digital thread be holistically traced from the point of demand to
fulfilment to completion without some kind of “middleware” or paper-based catalyst.
Decision-making tools (i.e., email, Microsoft Excel spreadsheets, PDFs, etc.) are
disaggregated and do not support automated workflows.
2) Database vice Automated Application. Logistics IT systems currently in inventory are
inherently complicated, lack automation, and are not connected (interfaced vice
integrated). Essentially, all these systems are a collection of siloed databases with an
abundance of free text fields not capitalizing on business process workflows, digital
signatures, or automated task behaviors. To align these multiple databases requires
human reconciliation and manual “swivel chair” methods to transcribe data between
systems.
3) Unfederated Data. Due to the multitude of IT systems and varying data schemas, all the
logistics data remains unfederated making it impossible to query data from different
sources into a single virtual environment. Additionally, as data is not within a shared
environment, data is copied repetitively from one data source to another data source to
another data source. This results in the generation, storage, and transmission of
duplicative and redundant data, thereby significantly increasing costs (i.e., infrastructure
to house that data) and demanding increased network resources—which may be
untenable in the aforementioned operating environment.
4) Limited Analytical Tools. None of the IT systems have a robust analytical framework to
assist in forecasting, identifying trends, prioritizing demands, consolidating similar
demands, capturing and sharing decision rationales, or supporting data-driven decision
making.
5) Needlessly Susceptible to Human Error. The manual nature of these IT systems
introduces a vast propensity for human error. These systems rarely have robust data
correction, validation, or business behavior rules applied which creates an unusually
permissive ability to input/ingest incorrect data.
2.4.1.4 As-is State of Logistics IT: Mission Impacts, Shortfalls, and Gaps
1) Inefficient use of Time. 80% of logistics administrative work time is spent manually
collecting data from multiple systems and/or filling in data gaps, 19% of work time is
spent analyzing or correcting data via “middleware” tools (including manual human
intervention), and 1% of work time is spent on decision making and/or action.4
2) Negates Commanders’ Decision Advantage. Logistics is the “pacing function”;
operations move at the speed of logistics. The slower the logistics, the slower the
commander’s decision-making cycle, and the slower decision-making cycle results in
decreased warfighter support and ultimately lethality.
3) Unacceptable Risk. These impacts, shortfalls, and gaps result in a very real risk to
mission accomplishment and human life, which can both be avoided. There is nothing
preventing the Marine Corps from solving these issues other than the will to do so and
unifying similar, disparate efforts across the enterprise.

4 Assessment of the LCE during MAGTF Warrior-17.

9

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
4) No Automated Data Inputs. The information systems are 100% reliant on human input
for identification, reporting, and updates. This is especially true in determining asset
existence and daily physical tracking. For example, on a quarterly basis, all owners and
responsible officers of assigned equipment must physically reconcile what is in their
possession with the automated report generated by Global Combat Support System –
Marine Corps (GCSS-MC). This is done by paper and pencil. Discrepancies are then
researched retroactively. Such reconciliations can be minimized or completely eliminated
through the implementation of technologies such as passive radio frequency
identification (pRFID), allowing for real time to near-real time tracking of assets thereby
providing a “digital truth.”
2.4.1.5 Study Initiation: Inherent Biases
This section provides transparency to the analysts’ cognitive biases upon initiation of this study
and throughout the course of this analysis. Recognizing this, these issues are not bound to
training, knowledge, small-unit leader, or command involvement constructs. Rather, this
problem space is defined by the methodologies supported—or not supported—by existing
logistics information systems. The plethora of disaggregated and isolated IT systems owned
and operated across the MCILE, at present, prevents the enterprise from fully capitalizing on
these investments.
The authors of this study hypothesized the following key problems to Marine Corps’ logistics
information and data management:5
1) Paper-based processes are the primary data capture tool.
2) When logistics processes are paper based, they are susceptible to human error and
incur significant time delay when transcribing/converting data into digital records, which
adversely impacts commanders’ decision-making advantage.
3) These logistics processes and their supporting IT systems are not integrated because
the enterprise does not have unity of effort in developing and/or deploying IT solutions.
4) The enterprise—as a whole—does not understand what data needs to be collected, for
what purpose, and at what level to initiate action in a contested environment. The
enterprise assumes the current collection of data is acceptable and relevant. This
observation is not an indictment on anyone or department but is a fact based on the
current state of logistics processes.
5) Neither the logistics processes or their IT systems are hierarchical in architecture, nor
are they holistic in design.
2.4.1.6 Assuming the Problem of Conditional Performance
The analysis assumes that shortfalls in current logistics information systems are compounded
by conditional performance. Conditional performance, in this context, is defined as the
employment of different processes and/or IT systems for analogous situations, due only to a
change in materiel, environment, or circumstance. For example, a logistics process in a
deployed environment for requesting fuel is significantly different than it is in garrison.
It is assumed the Marine Corps experiences conditional performance in two significant areas:
1) Application of different logistics processes for each of the different services or materiel
classifications. Some logistics processes are supported by IT, and some are not. Those
5 Based on the problems identified by the logisticians at various leadership roles the analysts attempt to

formulate a basic reason why these conditions exist

10

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
logistics processes that are supported by IT are not holistically integrated or capable of
supporting automation. A single unified logistics information system is absent.
2) The Marine Corps utilizes different systems in different operating conditions (i.e.,
deployed vs. garrison). When units deploy, they must adhere to a new set of logistics
processes designed specifically for the deployed environment. Garrison processes and
procedures do not migrate, on the whole, to the deployed environment. This was
especially true during Operations Enduring Freedom (OEF) and Iraqi Freedom (OIF)
where food, water, parts, and administrative supplies that were cataloged with an active
valid National Stock Number (NSN) were collectively ordered on a unified single courier
transaction. However, while in garrison, a separate process, system, and supply chain
exists for food, water, parts (consumable and secondary reparable), and administrative
supplies.
The myriad logistics processes and their associated information systems, coupled with the
conditional performance factor described above, illustrate challenges to current IT solution
priorities. The MCILE must refocus its attention on the operating environment.

2.4.2 Approach and Methodology
2.4.2.1 Approach Design: Applying DCOVA
Current operating environments are complex, especially so when considering the proliferation of
technology, IT systems and applications, methodologies, and other required capabilities. These
complexities demand an objective, scientific, systematic approach to address the problems
identified herein.
Establishing a business objective always precedes analysis, application of science, and
statistical modeling to logistics decision making. Completion of the problem framing phase,
triggers the application of the Define, Collect, Organize, Visualize, and Analyze (DCOVA)
framework. [8]
The DCOVA framework ensures the problem framing is systematic, identifies key relevant data
sources, and reduces complexity to the basic elements. DCOVA is comprised of five tasks: [8]
1) DEFINE the variables that require study to solve the problem or meet the objective.
2) COLLECT the data for the defined variables from authoritative data sources.
3) ORGANIZE the data collected by table, relevance, and relation.
4) VISUALIZE the organized data into charts, graphs, and diagrams to observe the effects,
contribution, and relation thereof to the problem.
5) ANALYZE the data collected by applying science, industry standards, and applicable
research methodologies to reach conclusions, make recommendations, and present
results.
Using this method, depicted in Figure 1, establishes quantitative and qualitative relevance to the
problem defined and can connect the logistics business decision to performance,
implementation, insight, and innovation.

11

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004

Figure 1: DCOVA Framework

2.4.2.2 Approach Questions
The following are questions that need to be answered or identify a path forward in determining
logistics data exchange requirements.
1) How do we provide for the integrity and availability of logistics information, and ensure its
secure and reliable exchange between the warfighter and the relevant combat service
support agency in a timely manner?
2) What does the current logistics operating paradigm look like?
3) What does the logistics concept of support NEED to look like to support future
operational scenarios? Has it, or will it change?
4) What does the current logistics IT system architecture look like? Does anybody know? Is
it different for each application?
5) What does that IT system architecture NEED to look like to enable logistics concepts of
support?
6) Can we leverage existing architectures?
7) Does a new solution need to be engineered entirely?
8) Can Information (or Data) Exchange Requirements (IER) be precisely defined?
9) What information needs to be communicated to whom and where—higher, adjacent,
subordinate, supporting, joint, coalition, contracted vendors?
2.4.2.3 Approach Methodology
1) Define. Five of the six functions of logistics were decomposed into their sub-functions—
as defined in doctrine—as the initial structure of this analysis. The variables are
comprised of the data elements in each of those categories.
2) Collect. Data points were collected for each function/sub-function. The system name,
purpose, and type were not relevant to the collection process and were omitted.
However, each data point was evaluated in its origin, echelon value, schema, authority,
and type.
3) Organize. The collected data points were organized by function/sub-function category.
The anticipated command/organizational echelon was then specified for each of those
data points.

12

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
4) Visualize. Once the collected data points were organized, a pivot table was created to
visualize how each data point aligned to its corresponding function/sub-function. Due to
the sheer volume of data points (approximately 1,000 in total), the full table is not
displayed below in Figure 2, but is included in Appendices B - F.

Figure 2: Functions of Logistics Visualization

5) Analyze. The analysis was tested and validated through a tabletop wargame. The
participants of the wargame were provided a realistic, current state scenario, intelligence
brief, and operation order, and given the task to identify the minimum data that must be
communicated to trigger required actions and facilitate the requested combat support.6

2.4.3 Structure of the Analysis
The structure of this analysis provides the operating context demanding a paradigm shift,
defines and scopes the problem, and identifies the preliminary findings and data commonalities.
Key terms and definitions are provided to ensure uniform, common understanding and their
contextual use throughout the analysis. Data exchange requirements for each function of
logistics (minus ‘Services’) is then presented. Lastly, critical capability gaps and recommended
changes to doctrine are proposed.

2.4.4 Reusability
This analysis is scalable and can be applied to multiple settings. The process and methodology
used herein offer an overall “how-to” structure and procedure. To reuse this analysis, the
analyst would:
1) Collect, read, and extract the required processes in the six functions of logistics, or any
project requiring data, from the library of directives, orders, and regulations as well as
observation of work in the six functions of logistics at each echelon. This collection is the
baseline of what is functionally required.
2) Extract and list current data requirements for each function and sub-function of logistics.
This process will reveal overlaps, redundancies, duplications, gaps, and help determine
the point of data creation/origin and subsequent curation into useable information.
3) Lastly, determine the echelon value, priority, and criticality of each data element in terms
of who, what, when, where, why, and how the data is curated and leveraged. Determine
the minimum data required for action/decision makers to begin their cycle/process for
support.

unclassified use.

Section 3: Tactical Logistics Data Analysis
3.1 Preliminary Findings Common to All Functions
3.1.1 Shared Data Elements
The participants of the Project ADRIAN wargame identified key data elements common to every
aspect of logistics, regardless of the function or sub-function. Traditionally, these common data
elements have varying degrees of granularity depending on the use.
3.1.1.1 Shared Observation of Data Relevance by Echelon
As data is developed upwardly, the granularity becomes less relevant. For instance, the serial
number of a truck identified for embark by the unit becomes less relevant as it moves upward in
the supporting chain as it is consolidated with other units’ embarkation requests. However, the
quantity, weight, and size are the enduring relevant data elements. The relevance condition
applies to all functions and sub-functions of logistics, without exception.
3.1.1.2 Differing Data Variations of “Who, What, When, Where, Why, and How”
Through the analytical process, it was determined that the basic data requirements were
common to each task but comprised of differing formats of the “five W’s and H”.
For instance, in terms of “who”, the Marine Corps captures this data for a unit/element as a
callsign, brevity code, Department of Defense Activity Address Code (DoDAAC), Unit
Identification Code (UIC), Monitored Command Code (MCC), organization name, Reporting Unit
Code (RUC), and in some cases Routing Identifier Code (RIC). Some of these elements are
hierarchical or nest into larger organizations but all primarily identify the element as “who” within
a system. Of note, some of these data variations are more akin to accounts, ledgers, and
balance sheets than truly element/unit identification, mission, purpose, and capability.
Each logistics IT system uses different forms of identification, location, and date/time formats
resulting in disparities in what should be commonly understood. These disparities produce data
gaps and forces data reinterpretation, thereby delaying decision cycle time and follow-on action.
3.1.1.3

Identical Data Replicated System to System

Since none of the data is federated from and to the highest authoritative source(s), current
logistics IT systems are not integrated, interoperable, or limited in system-to-system interface.
Basic data is generated, replicated, and copied repetitiously from authoritative sources to each
system independently.
Due to the lack of integration and interoperability, data becomes stale and unreliable as it
traverses between systems. To align data between systems, there is a systematic reliance on
human capital acting as system interfacing agents, consuming an enormous number of manhours to reconcile, validate, and transcribe data from one system to another. Additionally, the
Marine Corps has created and codified a culture of acceptance of human-based
reconciliation/validation through mandates, directives, and orders by making these actions
inspectable provisions that require proof via paper-based records. This creates conditions in
which Marines—as human capital—are working for the IT system(s) rather than the system
working for Marines.
This culture of acceptance places the burden of correcting data impurities and disparities on the
backs of the Marines, vice demanding system-wide changes to eliminate the need for data
reconciliations/validations in the first place. Systems lag in these improvements due to

14

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
perceived fiscal constraints (internally or externally imposed) in developing or correcting
information system flaws up-front.
3.1.1.4 Logistics Data Capture Approach
The analysis revealed that all logistics data is generated by analog sources. If data is captured
digitally, that data is transcribed from analog sources or back into analog sources. Logistics
processes exhibit a systematic reliance on paper correspondence to initiate action and
assign/respond to inquiries/requests, rather than leveraging information technology in the form
of business workflows. In tactical environments, data may be generated via chat applications
(i.e., mIRC, Mako, Transverse, Matrix), email, and verbally via voice-over-radio. In most
instances, if data is generated in a digital environment, it is then migrated to an analog
intermediary reporting source, processed via human “middleware”, and then transcribed into
another digital reporting system for decision/action. Data is transcribed from analog to digital
form by individual Marines—human beings—who manually enter data into specific fields of a
database or database-like system from paper sources of documentation (i.e., printed letters,
reports, forms, spreadsheets, etc.).
This human-dependent paradigm is inherently susceptible to data gaps and errors. Inaccurate,
erroneous, or irrelevant data is processed into misleading information with inconsistent variable
extrapolation. The Marine Corps, as an enterprise, does not have a clear and concise plan to
generate and capture data originating at the lowest echelons.
Despite the proliferation of new technology in recent years, the enterprise has yet to establish a
unified data collection plan. Lack of unity between the various logistics agencies, organizations,
and departments has only compounded this critical shortfall.
The result of not having a data capture plan, is that the enterprise does not consider or enable
where data originates at the most forward tactical edge, where the preponderance of actual
work and reporting is taking place.
For example, none of the maintenance lots, bays, stations, and benches in 3rd Marine Logistics
Group (MLG) have network access extended beyond their office spaces. The technology gap
results in lane non-commissioned officers (NCOs), technicians, operators, and mechanics to
develop analog stop-gap solutions. Marines on the tactical edge must typically transcribe
reporting data into an analog, organizationally unique form, and send it to a keying clerk for
manual input into the relevant logistics IT system. This problem is amplified in
exercise/deployed settings, causing the enduring reliance on paper logbooks as “true” data
systems of record.
If the Marine Corps logistics data capture plan does not consider the connectedness of devices
and/or connected/disconnected synchronization, every data-driven capture technology, no
matter how innovative, is degraded in its utility.

3.2 Key Terms and Definitions
In scientific and academic documentation, key terms and definitions are typically provided in a
glossary. However, organizations across the enterprise have used these terms inconsistently
and often deviate from their proper definitions. This section provides terms and definitions to
provide the appropriate context and establish common understanding throughout this analysis.

3.2.1 Defining ‘Logistics’
This section provides context to the term “logistics,” specifically, military logistics. While similar
to industry, military logistics encompasses greater scope. (i.e., human resources, engineering,

15

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
postal, etc.). The term “logistics” considers and recognizes the six doctrinal functions and
multitude of sub-functions, even though only five of the six are subject to this analysis.
When the term “logistics” is used, the analysis means this:

Figure 3: Operations to Logistics Relationship

To overcome the problem space defined in this analysis fill capability gaps, the logistics data
solution must holistically encompass all tasks associated within the five functions of logistics.
The solution must be homogeneous in terms of processes, data availability and accessibility,
and data exchange across the MCILE.
The Marine Corps has specified and directed how these functions will support and sustain the
force across the full spectrum of military operations on an operational and tactical level. It
should be noted, while Joint Publication 4-0—Joint Logistics (JP 4-0) and NATO Logistics
Handbook recognize seven functions, the Marine Corps only recognizes six (omission of
Operational Contract Support - OCS). [9] The continuance of this analysis will follow the Marine
Corps’ doctrine and omit the OCS function and relating tasks.

3.2.2 Defining ‘Contested’
For our military context, this means any action engaged in competition with or opposition to a
position of strength, power, or influence.
Great power competitors are now—at this time—engaged in a variety of actions to oppose U.S.
positions of strength, power, or influence in a variety of contexts, including operations,
communications, information, logistics, culture, unity of effort, or intelligence.

16

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
The term “contested logistics” invokes ideas of maritime shipping lanes and sustainment within
the first island chain of the western Pacific region once open conflict occurs. However, the
predominant near-peer competitor is already engaged in actions to challenge supply chain
power, position, and influence, not only regionally, but globally and within the U.S.

3.2.3 Defining ‘System’
The analysis requires a clear definition and context on how a system is defined and understood.
The following is a top-to-bottom summary of the components that comprise a ‘system’.

Figure 4: Step to Super System Hierarchy

1) Super System, or System-of-Systems: The assembly of multiple, independent systems
in context as part of a larger, more complex system (i.e., Logistics as a function is a
super system).
2) System: The collection of interconnecting processes organized within an operating
framework (i.e., the sub-function of ‘Supply’ is a system).
3) Process: A group of procedures taken in sequence to achieve a specific outcome.
4) Procedure: When a series of tasks/steps are conducted in a certain order.
5) Step or Task: The singular piece of work to be performed.
For this analysis, the super system is the warfighting function ‘logistics’, the sub-systems are the
six functions of logistics, and the processes are the collection of the series of actions or steps
taken to achieve the results, product, or end state desired within those logistics sub-functions.
The analysis attempts to avoid focusing on particular steps, as this type of detail detracts from
the context of the process of logistics at the functional level.

3.2.4 Defining ‘Capability’
For this analysis a ‘capability’ is defined as the quality, power, or ability in a present state to
efficiently/effectively accomplish something; the ability to do something.
To quantify a capability, however, three conditions must be met:
1) Alignment: A logistics capability must be specifically aligned to a function, and if
applicable, the appropriate subfunction; its purpose.
2) Enabling Factor: The logistics capability must enable a specific process, procedure, or
task.
3) Level: The logistics capability must be aligned to the echelon at which it is expected to
be performed or useful.
Below is an example of a quantifiable logistics capability.

17

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004

Figure 5: Properly Defined Capability Example

In this example the alignment is “materiel”, which specifically falls under the function of supply.
The enabling factor is “request”, and the level at which the capability is to be performed is “the
point of need or lowest tactical level”.

3.2.5 Defining ‘Kill Chain’
In the abstract, a ‘kill chain’ is defined as the signal pathway from “sensor” to “shooter” on the
battlefield.7 A ‘kill web’ is comprised of multiple kill chains linked together through shared
physical or logical signal paths.
Not every sensor needs to communicate with every shooter all the time. That model would
create congestion in the communications architecture and present an enormous force protection
risk when the adversary has significant electronic warfare (EW) capabilities.
“Shortening the kill chain” requires optimizing the signal pathway from “sensor” to “shooter” that
will enable effective action (kinetic or non-kinetic) in the quickest, most effective manner
possible. The right sensor communicates with the right shooter at the right time to achieve the
desired end state, and it must do so within a communications denied/degraded environment.
Therefore, a critical data analysis is required—that is, the minimum data exchange requirements
must be identified to facilitate the desired decision and/or action in each function of logistics.
Logistics IT must adopt this “kill chain/web” framework to enable effective decision making and
combat support in operational environments.
Applying this concept to logistics means the right “sensor”—human or technology device—
sending the right data to the right logistician at precisely the right time to initiate the required
decision and/or action.

3.2.6 Defining ‘Data’
Put simply, ‘data’ refers to individual facts and statistics collected for reference or analysis. In a
more granular sense, data represents the quantities, characters, or symbols on which
operations are performed by a computer, stored, and transmitted in the form of electrical signals
and recorded on magnetic, optical, or mechanical recording media.
For the purposes of this analysis data is not “information.” Data is the basic building block for
information. This analysis is focused on the “basement” level of data creation, and the purpose
for which it was created. Data at the lowest level allows all other capabilities to build upward.

7 The term “shooter” is not used exclusively to refer to kinetic fires assets. In the abstract, “shooter”

encompasses any system, node, or even human being that executes a task associated with the ingestion
of sensor data.

18

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004

Figure 6: Data Foundation to Wisdom Hierarchy

3.2.6.1 Data Types
For this analysis, the taxonomy of data types is defined as follows:
1) Data Field (Element): A container for the storage of a recorded fact, character, or
symbol. By and large, a data field/element is usually unusable in this basic state. A
collection of unrelated data fields is considered a “lake” of data.

Figure 7: Data Field (Element) Example

2) Data Block (Object): A logical, topic-based grouping of data fields (elements). This data
structure links individual data fields/elements into something more useful.

Figure 8: Data Block (Object) Example

3) Data File: A collection of data blocks (objects) on which operations, analysis, and/or
calculations are performed at a higher level. This is where information begins to take
shape.

Figure 9: Data File Example

19

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
3.2.6.2 Data Significance
The majority of data collected in the Marine Corps today is input/created at the data
transactional layer, also known as the transaction management foundation. Data
creators/inputters are, more often than not, 18 – 22-year-old human beings—the Private First
Class (PFC) and Corporal (Cpl) population at the beginning of their military careers.
Anyone above the data transactional layer is a “data consumer”. Data consumers are divorced
of data creation. Additionally, there is currently no means by which to ensure that data—
manually input by a human being—is correct, accurate, timely, and verified upon its entry into
the system.
Strategic decisions at the highest echelons are therefore based on data generated at the
lowest echelons and manually input into permissive logistics IT systems by the most
junior population of the Marine Corps’ workforce.

3.2.7 Defining ‘Knowledge Management’
Data is the nucleus of knowledge. Data must be collected, processed, and developed for it to
have value. This section explains the importance and impacts of this science as it pertains to
logistics data, how it is exchanged, and what is required for it to enable action/decision.
3.2.7.1 Military Application of Knowledge Management
Knowledge management (KM) in theoretical, scientific, and academic circles has only recently
emerged as a body of science. While not a new concept, the KM framework is relatively
immature in the DoD, with only 20 years compared to more than 200 years of other military
disciplines. [10]
KM must not be conflated with information management (IM) and should be addressed. In many
instances these terms are often used interchangeably, however, they differ in both meaning and
content. The following sources provide a basic point of reference that illustrate that disparity of
definitions between the service departments: [10]
1) Marine Corps Doctrinal Publication (MCDP) 2 – Intelligence. Describes information as
“unevaluated material of any kind… [used as] raw material from which intelligence is
derived.”8 From this perspective, information is data that can be processed and put into
an understandable form. It is the basic building block of knowledge and understanding
and is essential to intelligence. [11]
2) MCDP 6 – Command and Control. Describes information as “…all manner of
descriptions or representations from raw signals on the one hand, to knowledge and
understanding on the other.”9 In this context, information categories range from data—
raw or unprocessed signals—to more highly developed forms evaluated/integrated into
meaningful knowledge and understanding. [12]
3) Department of the Navy Knowledge Management Strategy. The DoN Chief Information
Officer (CIO) defines KM as “the integration of people and processes, enabled by
technology, to facilitate the exchange of operationally relevant information and expertise
to increase organizational performance.” [13]

8 Page 1-7
9 Page 66

20

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
For the purposes of this analysis, ‘knowledge’ management is defined as follows:
The process and ability of an organization to capture, develop, share, and effectively use
knowledge as it pertains to the organization’s mission, purpose and/or goal. [14]10
This definition is the foundation upon which the efficacy/efficiency of data supporting logistics
processes and measures of effectiveness thereof are determined. This may be a concept to
which the Marine Corps is not accustomed, and is outside the normative institutional emphasis
on training, leadership, and command influence to resolve KM issues.
Knowledge requires data that is timely, accurate, and factually sound. That data must be
thoroughly examined in the context of the ‘kill chain’ framework for it to be useful, efficient, and
effective.
3.2.7.2 Data Development in the ‘Data, Information, Knowledge, Wisdom (DIKW)’
Framework
Developing basic elements of data into something useful requires two conditions to be met:
connectedness and understanding.
1) Connectedness indicates the level of accessibility and reliability of the data as it is
transformed through understanding (i.e., a shared environment or integrated system),
commonly facilitated through systems interconnected with one another via
communications network pathways (i.e., internet, intranet, cloud platforms, etc.).
2) Understanding is the application of cognitive and analytical processes to synthesize new
knowledge from previously held beliefs. [15] Understanding applies to both tacit and
explicit knowledge.11 12
The chart below depicts data development within the framework of connectedness and
understanding.

Figure 10: Data Development Framework

Applying the data development framework reveals the necessary relationships between
connectedness and understanding required to mature data and transition it through the
hierarchy. This framework provides fundamental insights into what it takes to turn raw data into
something useful.

10 Original definition contextualized to fit military applications.
11 Tacit knowledge is a kind of knowledge that is difficult to transfer via written or verbal form. It must be

gained and accumulated primarily through experience. [17]
12 Explicit knowledge is the kind of knowledge that can be transferred through written or verbal forms. It
can be gained through codification or formalization as well as academic education. [17]

21

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
1) Data. Data is the foundation of the DIKW framework, and usually exists as a transaction
or entry. [16] Data is a discrete element; it is distinctly individual and unconnected.
Connectedness must be in a shared data environment. Paper-based or multiple stovepiped information systems will always impede data movement through the hierarchy. An
understanding of relationships is required for data to be processed and synthesized into
information. Data quality at this level is imperative and has an upstream effect on all
levels of this hierarchy. In a shared data environment, bad data leads to bad decisions—
“garbage in, garbage out”. This is why many organizations invest in linear transactional
standardization with little area to deviate. In industry, deviations from transactional data
inputs usually require a supervisor to override in special circumstances and must be
justified.
2) Information. When data points are contextualized and compared, then data has
transitioned to the information level in the hierarchy. [16] Usually, information comes in the
form of reports or listings in which separate data points can be compared within a certain
context. Connectedness contributes through interfaced or integration of multiple systems
so data points can be arranged to provide meaning. In order to move from information to
knowledge, an understanding of patterns or trends must be established. Information
usually looks to the past, relative to the present.
3) Knowledge. Information arranged to show/visualize a trend or pattern has transitioned to
knowledge. [16] Knowledge commonly manifests as dashboards, graphs, and charts,
which are employed to indicate a basis by which to measure performance.
Connectedness influences knowledge by presenting information from different-butrelated data sources. To move from knowledge to wisdom, an understanding of
principles and/or theories must be applied. Knowledge usually looks to real-time or nearreal-time in relation to the present.
4) Wisdom. Formulated knowledge that is analytical in nature can be used to forecast
future events or situations. [16] Wisdom enables decision within time of opportunity,
creating a “decision advantage.” Thus, wisdom becomes time-sensitive; as the lower
hierarchal levels change, so does the wisdom affecting the decision of opportunity.
Connectedness supports wisdom by providing real-time or near-real-time updates
across multiple but related data points. Wisdom looks to determine future outcomes
based on current developments and are continually changing as updates occur.
Data becomes more strategic in nature the higher in the DIKW hierarchy it moves. Individual
transactional data detail becomes less relevant as knowledge becomes more directional in
nature (i.e., serial number of an asset becomes less relevant compared to quantity of assets the
further the data is developed). The graph below depicts the hierarchies and the type of
understanding required to transition from one stage to the next.

22

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004

Figure 11: Data Development Hierarchy

The process of knowledge management is essential to apply the science to logistics information
systems as the process frames the general goal of development of data in these systems.
When comparing the components of knowledge management, an “intelligent” (either machine
assisted or artificially enhanced) based system requires a unified system which incorporates the
subject’s theory to provide an analytically based forecast on future outcomes. The table below
shows the knowledge management requirements at each level in order to achieve the ideal
state of each.

3.2.7.4 Knowledge Management as a Demand for Logisticians
Previous sections have detailed the evolution and drivers of information technology in the
logistics sector. For military applications, what has changed in the knowledge management
context? As in industry, the military is now beginning to see the comparative difference in
‘knowledge work’ and more traditional ‘manual work’. Unlike repetitive tasks generally
associated with manual work, knowledge work involves the cognitive abilities of the human
brain.
Manual Work Is:
Highly visible
Highly specialized
Stable
Fixated on the right answers
Focused on structure with few decisions
