→
→
→
→

Knowledge Work Is:
Largely invisible
Holistic
Ever-changing
Fixated on the right questions
Focused on less structure with more decisions

Table 4: Manual Work vs. Knowledge Work

KM shares many of the same characteristics as what the Marine Corps would call “smart staff
work”, the fundamentals of staff action which have existed as long as our Armed Forces. In
March 2014, the DoN CIO reissued an updated KM strategy outlining a vision to “… to create,
capture, share, and reuse knowledge to enable effective and agile decision-making, increase
the efficiency of task accomplishment, and improve mission effectiveness.” [17] This DoN KM
Strategy outlines four primary goals:
1) Goal 1: Continue to expand Departmental awareness that KM applied to operational and
business processes will enable significant improvements in mission accomplishment.
2) Goal 2: Instill KM principles and methodologies into the DoN culture.
3) Goal 3: Maximize the utility of existing DoN KM experience and resources.
4) Goal 4: Drive towards a more centrally supported universally available and executed
DoN KM.
Overall, the DoN has identified KM opportunities to adopt and mature communications,
knowledge sharing, and collaboration to promote an increase staff productivity by 20 to 25
percent. [18]13
Since a large percentage of military staff work is knowledge-based, especially that of
logisticians, it is essential to increase the productivity of the logistician as a primary means of
responding to increased mission demands and decreased personnel/financial resources. In
other words, technology must be leveraged in a way that gives time and money back to the unit
where velocity in the logistics chain is based on data flow; or, alternatively, leveraging ITenabled automation to fill the resource gap.
In order for the benefits of the above strategy to be realized, it is necessary to understand the
four basic information characteristics which facilitate productivity through data-based logistics
technology. These characteristics drawn from industry do in fact apply to government/military
applications [19]:
13 “…the McKinsey Global Institute (MGI) finds that twice as much potential value lies in using social tools

to enhance communications, knowledge sharing, and collaboration within and across enterprises. MGI’s
estimates suggest that by fully implementing social technologies, companies have an opportunity to raise
the productivity of interaction workers—high-skill knowledge workers, including managers and
professionals—by 20 to 25 percent.”

25

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
1) Information accuracy. Good decisions are difficult or nearly impossible without
information that gives a true picture of the state of logistics. Information does not need to
be 100% precise all the time, but rather the data available must paint a picture that is
directionally accurate. The more automated the system, the more accurate information
becomes.
2) Information accessibility. Decision makers need data at the right time, in the right place,
and it must be accessible. This is the application of the “kill chain” model. Most times
accurate data may exist but by the time it becomes available (by transcribing from
analog to digital or interfacing) it is either out of date or is not accessible in the operating
environment. The more integrated the system (vice interfacing) the more accessible the
system becomes.
3) Information type. Organizations typically capture vast amount of data. However, the
quantity of data captured does not ensure good decision making. Decision makers
require information that is relevant to the decision they are making. Therefore,
enterprises must think about the data that is recorded, how it is recorded, and if it should
be recorded at all so that valuable resources are not wasted collecting meaningless data
while important data is not being recorded. This is the essential component of federated
data.14
4) Information sharing. Logistics can only be effective if all organizations are sharing a
common view of the information used to make decisions. Different information with
different sub-organizations results in misaligned action plans that hamper logistic chain
performance. Data sharing must occur horizontally (lateral) as well as vertically
(hierarchical). It should be noted paper-based systems do not lend themselves to
information sharing in any form and at any level. Paper-based methods must be used as
a last resort in the direst of circumstances and only temporarily.
Therefore, we can conclude information, as represented as knowledge management, is the
lifeline of the logistics chain process which drives effective decisions and actions on data
exchange requirements [20]. The criticality of logistics information to the logistician is essential as
their direct line of sight to logistics processes is very limited – this is especially true for the SIF
concept which operates in an austere distributed operating environment.

3.3 Logistics Data Elements and Interdependencies
Through analysis and the LOG IT Wargame, it became evident there exists essential data
requirements common to all functions, agnostic to the type of request (material, information, or
service) or if the request is push or pull or echelon level of origination. These essential data
elements provide context and must endure through the lifecycle of the request providing trace
back.
The perspective all the following data requirements is from the lowest tactical user leveraging
the demand.

14 A federated database is virtual and does not physically store any data. Instead, it provides an interface

where users can query information across multiple data sources and coordinates information across noncongruent systems.

26

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004

3.3.1 Solution to Voice-over-Radio Communications
3.3.1.1 Legacy Radio Communications
Pre-formatted military reports are designed and geared towards voice-over-radio
communications. These pre-formatted reports have changed little since field radios were first
used by the United States Army in the Spanish American War of 1898 and the Philippine
Insurrection of 1899–1902. The standardization of the reports ensures the recipient has the right
data for the purpose of the communication. Even so, transmitting voice-over-radio reports and
requests require military phonetic spelling of key data elements with the repetitive transmitting
and confirmation repeating of data. Voice-over-radio transmissions exposes forces in the
following ways:
1) Near-peer competitors have the ability to scan, detect, and triangulate EM
transmissions, including radio frequency transmissions. Every time the handset is
repetitively keyed, the radio transmits exposing forces to detection by pattern.
2) The time it takes to transmit, even the simplest of reports/demands, via voice-over-radio
because of the phonetic spelling of each data element elongates the electromagnetic
detection exposure risk. Even through the method of spelling, reiteration, and
confirmation the message is subject to human error and mis-transliteration.
3) Voice-over-radio transmissions are verbally passed data elements. These elements are
transcribed onto various paper forms, logbooks, etc. for processing. Again, paper-based
forms of data capture are the least reliable form of data capture.
Voice-over-radio in this competition continuum must be the very last option for communication.
3.3.1.2 Using High Frequency (HF) for Data Transmission
HF Radio is slow, with bandwidth ranging from 75 to 9600 bits per second, with a typical rate of
1200 bits per second. However, such HF radios can achieve worldwide radio coverage when
propagation conditions are right by bouncing signals off the ionosphere. If the analysis assumes
the farthest forward, smallest tactical unit has access to organic Marine Corps Falcon III
AN/PRC-160 radio systems, they will have the reach, but the limitation of data speed transport
still remains. Therefore, data exchange requirements need a complete overhaul to scale down
the data transmission size. Data no longer needs to be in the legacy voice-over-radio template.

Figure 12: HF to HF Data Transmission

Data processing with a data burst capability across the available communication networks must
be the primary method. Data burst is a small compact transmission limited only by the radio
carrier wavelength. The quality checking by the hardware/software combination ensures

27

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
trustworthy connectedness through data “handshake” and checks. With a shorter transmit time
and quality data assurance, the risk to forces is minimized without feeding the enemy’s kill chain
but the data passed must be the absolute critical elements.

3.3.2 Data Focus: User Input and Request Data
The following is focusing on the forward tactical user in a communication constrained
(intermittent) environment. This is the basis of providing the critical data elements to generate a
request across the five of six functions of logistics and its sub-functions. The following will
challenge previous held positions on data requirements, flow, and accessibility/retrieval.

Figure 13: Data Focus: User Input to a Request

3.3.3 Request Header Concept
The request header concept is the embedded data populated at the time of request creation
with little to no user interaction. Header data is an integral part of the data exchange
requirements especially for disconnected resynchronization and/or limited bandwidth. The
header precedes the actual request information and initiates the demand process as well as
providing a common context for follow-on information gathering, status, and response from
supporting activities.

Figure 14: Data Cycle for Requests

3.3.3.1 Request Header Data Elements
The following is the required data elements to be embedded into the header of request initiation
and are not input by the user. The user has no direct interaction or control over the header data
elements.
1) System Date Format – Local. Date expressed as CCYYMMMDD.

28

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
2) System Time Format – Local. System time expressed in 24-hour clock time as follows:
HHMM, or HHMMSS, or HHMMSSD, or HHMMSSDD, where H = hours (00-23), M =
minutes (00-59), S = integer seconds (00-59) and DD = decimal seconds; decimal
seconds are expressed as follows: D = tenths (0-9) and DD = hundredths (00-99). Used
to ensure non-duplication of requests. (Note: Decimal seconds are required for the
header in order to prevent duplicate time stamps on separate transactions.)
3) Universal Time Coordinate (UTC) Time (Calculated-Data not Passed). In accordance
with International Standards Organization (ISO) standard 8601, time can be specified by
a + or - and an indication in hours in relation to Universal Time Coordinate (UTC) time;
local time is calculated as UTC (plus or minus hours/minutes). Since + is a restricted
character, + and – can be substituted by P (plus) and M (minus).
4) Military Date/Time Group (DTG) (Calculated-Data not Passed). DTG expressed as UTC
Time/Date: DD HHMM (SS) Z MMM YY based and calculated off the system date and
time.
5) System Synchronized Geospatial Reference. Expressed as last system synchronized
reference in Military Grid Reference System (MGRS). Position 1-2: Alpha (Grid Zone
Designator, GZD), Position 3-4: Alpha (100K-meter Square Identifier), Position 5-14:
Numeric (Numerical Location) depending on accuracy level within 1-, 10-, and 100-meter
intervals: position 5-9: Numeric (Easting) and position 10-14: Numeric (Northing).
Remarks: Physical location does not require delivery to this location but serves as a
reference in data collection and origination history which can correlate to unit/element
use.
6) Local Key Place Holder. Unique system temporarily assigned local key place holder until
request is connected, acknowledged, and synchronized to the enterprise system. The
device will generate a unique 10-digit alpha-numeric place holder for transmission. Once
synchronized, this key is replaced.
7) Request Tracking Identification. Enterprise system unique generated. The enterprise
assigned tracking number can be assumed to be an alpha-numeric randomly assigned
12-digit ID. A 12-digit alpha-numeric ID with 24 alpha characters (removing “O” and “I”)
and ten numeric characters (zero through nine) offers 2,704,222,032,852,670,000
unique combinations (over 2 quintillion combinations) therefore, the chance of a
duplicated request ID is statistically impossible.
8) Request Priority. Expressed as a two-digit numerical input. Priority assignment
throughout the logistics system is based on the Uniform Materiel Movement and Issue
Priority System (UMMIPS) on the unit/element Force Activity Designator (F/AD) based
on the Operational Need Goals (ONG) delineated by mission requirements. Priority
government is correlated from the assigned F/AD.
Force/Activity Designator (F/AD)

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
Priorities are pre-loaded based on the unit/element’s F/AD. The data element is
populated by the segment’s priority. If the request has multiple segments with differing
priorities, the highest priority has precedence.
9) Element/Unit Identification/Call Sign. Expressed as a four-digit alpha-numeric reference
key which aligns to the clear text naming convention. “Clear text” is written in normal
English, as visible to the user, but the information is stored and transmitted using a
unique code. An example in this case, is “1st Platoon, Transportation Services Company,
Combat Logistics Battalion 4” is displayed to the user, but the information system
transmits the unit identifier data as “G8AQ”. The clear text naming convention does not
need to be passed, only the unique index key which was randomly assigned by system.
The origination of callsigns/brevity codes for units/elements is with the operations
function. This stresses the importance of operation planning and execution occurring in a
digital environment.
Alpha-numeric combination of a 4-digit key could be a combination as follows:
a. 4 alpha characters, 0 numeric characters (4 to 0)
b. 3 alpha characters, 1 numeric character (3 to 1)
c. 2 alpha characters, 2 numeric characters (2 to 2)
d. 1 alpha character, 3 numeric characters (1 to 3)
e. 0 alpha characters, 4 numeric characters (0 to 4)
The alpha characters are not case sensitive. While there are 26 letters in the alphabet,
the letters “O” and “I” are removed from the available combinations as not to be
confused with “zero” and “one” leaving 24 letters for the unique key.
Numeric characters are zero through nine providing 10 optional characters in the key.
Available alpha-numeric unit identification/call sign combinations provide 1,080,016
unique keys.

10) Request Segments. Expressed as a single numeric digit from one to nine. This means
the maximum allowable requests is limited to nine but must be greater than zero. This
does not restrict the number of lines within the request segment itself.
The request segment is used for data verification to ensure the full data file has been
passed and corresponds to children requests within the main request container.

3.3.3.3 Request Header Schema
The following is the data schema of the exchanged data segments which would be passed part
of the initial communication data burst.
Header data schema format:
CCYYMMMDD-HHMMSSDD-XXXX123456-XXXXXXXXXX-00-XXXX-00
Header data schema example:
2027OCT13-15470352-4QFJ123456-3838JBNM5-02-KL9K-01
Table 8: Header Data Schema and Example

The example header portrays a request generated on 13 October 2027 at 15:47 for a single
request which is priority 02 (critical) for a unit corresponding to unit/activity ID KL9K. The last
synchronized physical location is embedded along with the temporary request ID. The header
text size is equivalent to 50 bits.
Upon synchronization the temporary request ID is replaced, and the local machine notes the
date/time of synchronization.
Synchronized response data:
3838JBNM5-KL9K15474QFJ
Table 9: Header Synchronized Data

The data received from the enterprise replaces the local machine ten-digit tracking ID
(3838JBNM5) with the enterprise assigned 12-digit tracking ID (KL9K15474QFJ).

3.4 Transportation Data Requirements
The transportation data requirements presented in this section are based on the future doctrinal
changes proposed in Section 4 and will not be discussed here. While the header and title of this

31

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
section refers to the legacy term for this function as ‘Transportation’, throughout this section the
function will be referred to as ‘Mobility’.
Each of the following data requirements are the request segments which are attached to the
request header. Request header information will not be duplicated within the request segments.

Table 10: Mobility Cycle

3.4.1 Functional Interdependencies
3.4.1.1 Supply Function
The data requirements for the embarkation of people and things (cargo) are interdependent with
the ‘Requirements’ sub-function of Supply, which dictate the generation of equipment density
lists (EDL) and manning/staffing documents. Any variation in the element’s staffing and
equipment level compared to its established structure becomes a staffing document and an
EDL.
Output data files of the staffing and EDL hold the critical unit or element data. It is not
anticipated forward deployed units would have the entirety of these files on the local machine
but only the data slice which pertains to the unit/element directly.
Data slices ensure the minimum retrievable data is encapsulated and stored locally, reducing
potential surface exploitation vectors. Regardless, the embark data is wholly dependent on data
anchored in the staffing and EDL, found in the ‘Requirements’ sub-function of ‘Supply’.
The user making the request will need to have access to their specific data on the local
machine. This data slice should be apportioned and scaled to the appropriate level. For
example, the EDL is apportioned from the original structure found in Total Force Structure
Management for allowances and the supply system for on hand quantities, the EDL would be
apportioned into a consolidated memorandum receipt (CMR), which would be apportioned
further into mission equipment or gear lists and/or individual issue lists.
3.4.1.2 Maintenance Function
‘Mobility’ is interdependent on the ‘Maintenance’ function for the operational classification of its
assets to perform its combat mission. The role of maintenance is to determine what conditions
meet the standards of the primary role the asset is to perform (i.e., shoot, move, or
32

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
communicate). Mobility as owner/operators is limited in the capacity to effect critical restorative
repairs and is reliant of the maintenance function to provide the current classification
(operational, degraded, or deadline), but also to correct the defects to return the asset to an
operational state.
3.4.1.3 General Engineering
Mobility requires the services from General Engineering in terms of providing area, zone, and
route reconnaissance for all modes of transportation. General Engineering provides surveys and
measurements from roads, bridges, bypasses, bi-ways, highways, surf, shore, beach, landing
zones, runways, etc., without which Mobility cannot be successfully executed.

3.4.2 Processing Cycle at the Functional Level
Once the mobility request is received, the request is processed for additional decision or action.
The data requirements for action/decision after the request has been initiated are not within
scope of this analysis. However, the general process is worth noting for future development.
3.4.2.1 Capacity Planning
The next step in demand fulfillment for movement of PAX and/or cargo is capacity planning. The
data provided on the request is evaluated against available and accessible platforms according
to time, weight, and destination/origination constraints. Capacity planning occurs at all echelons
and only changes in scale and scope. Capacity planning has a dependency on at least two
distinct data sets: what is available, and what is requested.
3.4.2.2 Transportation Mode Selection
Once capacity planning has compared the platform capacity/availability data with the demand
data, the best mode of transportation is selected. The original request may spawn a related
request or be consolidated with similar requests. Once the mode of transportation is selected it
is then assigned to the movement execution activity.
3.4.2.3 Movement Execution
Movement execution will respond to the capacity planning assignment through manifesting,
dispatching, monitoring, and tracking the physical movement. Additionally, the movement
execution will assign landing support as required depending on the conditions of the mission
(permissive vs. non-permissive). Movement execution will append additional data (funding data,
MHE requests, etc.) to the original/supplemental request(s) as required until the mission is
complete, at which time the request is closed.

3.4.3 Data Exchange Requirements
The embark data requirements presented here are based on the lowest level tactical unit
requesting movement. This is a departure from the traditional paradigm, wherein embarkation is
associated with the movement of mass formations to support an operation or exercise. In that
legacy model, smaller movement requests and their corresponding level 4 (nested) and level 6
(mobile/loaded items) data are classified as “transportation requests” or “logistics requests”,
thereby bypassing the core competencies of the unified execution of embark, distribution, and
transportation mode selection.
This analysis revealed that there is little variation in required data between smaller and larger
embarkation movements. Because frequent, smaller movement requests are generated in
analog forms, that data is frequently lost.

33

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
The minimum data requirements for the embarkation of PAX/cargo are ubiquitous regardless of
scale and scope of movement (i.e., from the fire team level to Marine Expeditionary Brigade
(MEB)).
3.4.3.1

PAX Request Data

The PAX segment identifies the people requiring movement from one place to another. It is
agnostic to transportation mode as this occurs during capacity planning.
1) Segment Number: System assigned, as the request is created under the header. A
single digit indicator; up to nine independent request types can be added under the
header but does not restrict the number of individual lines contained in the request.
2) Request Type: Expressed as a two-digit background code when the user selects “PAX
Movement”. In this case PM is the code for a PAX movement.
3) Request priority – Expressed as two-digit code based on the urgency aligned to the
element/unit’s F/AD.
4) ZAP/EDI-PI: Expressed as either 8 to 9-digit ZAP or 10-digit EDI-PI. ZAP data element is
a pre-formatted and system-assigned abbreviation for USMC personnel. EDI-PI is DoDassigned for all DoD personnel (active, reserve, government service (GS), and
contractor). User should not necessarily need to type in these identifiers but select from
a preloaded list on the local machine or scan a government issued identification card if
the individual is not in the list.

used for capacity planning and transportation mode selection. Certain precautions must
be taken depending on the response of hazardous material being hand carried or
expected to be in the passenger area of the supporting platform.

to/from across the communication pathway.
12) Total Baggage Weight: Calculated. Expressed as the sum of all baggage weight
identified in the request segment. As this is a calculated field, the data does not need to
be passed to/from across the communication pathway.

3.4.3.2 Cargo Request Data
Cargo requests are used for the movement of military materiel. A Cargo request is not primarily
used for personal baggage, while it could be, it is not the intent.
1) Segment Number. System assigned as the request is created under the header. A single
digit indicator: up to nine independent request types can be added under the header.
2) Request Type. Expressed as a two-digit background code when the user selects “Cargo
Movement”. In this case CM is the code for a PAX movement.
3) Request Priority. Expressed as two-digit code based on the urgency aligned to the
element/unit’s F/AD.
4) Item by NIIN. When the user selects the serial number or the clear text of the item(s) to
be shipped, the system will populate the North Atlantic Treaty Organization (NATO) Item
Identification Number (NIIN) into the request segment. The NIIN is a code in and of itself.
5) Item Quantity. If the cargo does not have serialization, the quantity of item(s) can be
input.
6) Serial Number. User selects from the pre-populated list from the gear list and/or EDL. If
the serial number is selected, the NIIN will auto populate. Serial numbers are counted as
(1) per item.
7) Gross Weight. The total weight, in pounds, of the pallet, box, or end item being shipped.
8) Actual Height. The actual measured height in whole inches.
9) Actual Width. The actual measured width in whole inches.
10) Actual Length. The actual measured length in whole inches.
11) Hazardous Material Indicator Code (HMIC). A non-user inputted single (1) character
alphabetic code used to identify all known or suspected hazardous items as defined by
safety, health, transportation and/or environmental regulations to serve as a flag that an
activity must acquire a Material Safety Data Sheet (MSDS) to inform Federal Catalog
System users when more detailed information on an item is available in the Hazardous
Material Information System. The HMIC is related to the NIIN, once the NIIN is accepted
into the enterprise connected system, the HMIC will populate. D - There is no
information in the Hazardous Materials Information Reporting System (HMIRS);
however, the National Stock Number (NSN) is in a Federal Stock Class (FSC) in Table I
of Federal Standard 313 and an MSDS should be available to the user. N - There is no
data in the HMIRS and the NSN is in an FSC not generally suspected of containing
hazardous materials. P - There is no information in the HMIRS; however, the NSN is in
an FSC in Table II of Federal Standard 313 and an MSDS may be required by the user.
The requirement for an MSDS is dependent on a hazard determination of the supplier or
the intended end use of the product. Y - Information is found in HMIRS.
12) Handling. Expressed as a single alphabetic code from the user’s clear text selection to
indicate special handling at the point of destination: C – Crane or Hoist; M – MHE; T –
Tow; R – Ramp; X – None
13) Earliest Departure Date - Local. Expressed as local date CCYYMMMDD assuming
midnight to 23:59 as a full day. The first full day available day for departure.
14) Latest Departure Date - Local. Expressed as local date CCYYMMMDD assuming
midnight to 23:59 as a full day. The last full day available day for departure.

36

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
15) Departure Location. Geospatial reference expressed in Military Grid Reference System
(MGRS). Position 1-2: Alpha (Grid Zone Designator, GZD), Position 3-4: Alpha (100Kmeter Square Identifier), Position 5-14: Numeric (Numerical Location) depending on
accuracy level within 1-, 10-, and 100-meter intervals: position 5-9: Numeric (Easting)
and position 10-14: Numeric (Northing). Remarks: Physical can be pre-loaded as a
predetermined clear text descriptor (i.e., White Beach, Pier 3, etc.) for ease of use.
16) Destination Location. Geospatial reference expressed in Military Grid Reference System
(MGRS). Position 1-2: Alpha (Grid Zone Designator, GZD), Position 3-4: Alpha (100Kmeter Square Identifier), Position 5-14: Numeric (Numerical Location) depending on
accuracy level within 1-, 10-, and 100-meter intervals: position 5-9: Numeric (Easting)
and position 10-14: Numeric (Northing). Remarks: Physical can be pre-loaded as a
predetermined clear text descriptor (i.e., MCAS Miramar, etc.) for ease of use.
17) Item Count – Calculated. Expressed as the sum of items identified in the request
segment. As this is a calculated field, the data does not need to be passed to/from
across the communication pathway.
18) Total Weight – Calculated. Expressed as the sum of the weight identified in the request
segment. As this is a calculated field, the data does not need to be passed to/from
across the communication pathway.

3.5 Supply Data Requirements
The analysis determined, the function of supply is a multi-faceted operation relying heavily on
data being communicated routinely to establish demand signals, response by status, and
reporting of progress. Furthermore, due to the close proximity of the mobility function to
successfully execute supply fulfillment, this has led to the misconception of “distribution” being
“transportation” and thereby, supply being wholly responsible for the physical movement of
supplies and materiel in the transportation network/pipeline. Therefore, this analysis aims to
retain the functional boundary between mobility and distribution within the supply function and
emphasize the importance of data sharing, availability, and accessibility in a unified, singular
logistics information system.
Additionally, the supply function has been greatly compartmentalized around classification of
supplies. Each of the ten classes of supply have established one or more distinct, separate, and
specialized methods of requesting, record keeping, accounting, access requirements, logistics
IT support (if any), and independent supply chains. These factors demand both the customer
and the supporting organization supply personnel to be somewhat proficient in navigating these
artificially manufactured and self-imposed processes and systems. This analysis presents the
data exchange requirements from the perspective of the warfighter. The analysis determined
the warfighter should not be required to navigate – or be subjected to – the current established
complexities for communication of materiel requests even if the enterprise continues to hold on
to these self-imposed structures.

38

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004

Figure 15: Supply Cycle

3.5.1 Functional Interdependencies
3.5.1.1 Mobility
There exists a partnership between the two functions of supply and mobility to ensure the
customer’s requirement is fulfilled. This partnership requires coordination, data sharing, and
mutual accountability since requisitioning is a two-part process: 1) fulfillment 2) transportation to
customer.
The supply function is responsible for the first part of the requisitioning process, which is
determining fulfilment method, inventory management, and stocking. Once the demand has
been fulfilled the second part of requisitioning is transportation which is the function of mobility.
3.5.1.2 Maintenance
Supply activities rely on the doctrinal subfunction of maintenance in classification of repair as
well as maintenance related physical preparatory work in the area of disposal/salvage
operations. Maintenance may classify equipment as unrepairable due to cost, lack of resources,
obsolesce or extended maintenance cycle time. Supply’s functional role is to administratively
communicate the item identification information, request higher directed disposition/actions,
identify unique demilitarization requirements, and report the completion of actions. However,
Marines performing supply actions do not perform the physical preparatory work and any
demilitarization actions on the item itself, this separation of actions requires unique information
to be shared for successful completion.

3.5.2 Processing Cycle at the Functional Level
Supply is the most visible link between the operations warfighting function and the logistics
warfighting function. The direct inject of operational data (desired capabilities, intent, etc.)
become the sourcing requirements in the supply function.
3.5.2.1 Requirements Determination
Once the operational order is issued, the supply function begins the requirements
determination. There are no logistics IT systems dedicated to this sub-function. Requirements
determination is comprised of three subordinate functions:

39

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
1) Planning. Determining what the operational objective requires both in terms of
equipment and personnel for the duration of the objective.
2) Allowancing. Sets the required allowance of equipment to meet the operational
objective. Allowance pertains primarily to equipment but also has an application to
people as well commonly referred to as ‘staffing’ or ‘manning’.
3) Forecasting. The sustainment based on the allowancing factor. Forecasting is
determining future requirements and is accomplished through the application of various
scientific principles by adjusting various variable sets that account for atmospherics,
mobility, environment, etc. Forecasting can range from rudimentary to complex. Even
with the best intentions, forecasting cannot foresee and anticipate every situation and
therefore, requires the customer ability to request materiel to fill the forecast gap.
3.5.2.2 Distribution
Distribution executes the ability to identify, assign, and dispense the materiel, equipment, and/or
supplies as well as assign military personnel to identified activities, units, and/or billets. This
function must determine if the assets required to the fill the established allowances are currently
on hand (within the inventory), if what is on hand is available (operational), and if these assets
are accessible (can be physically obtained and employed). Depending on the answers to these
variables sets, the next set of actions by the distribution action are as follows:
1) If the assets are on hand, available, and accessible, then such assets can be allocated
from inventory by assigning, issuing, or dispensing.
2) If the assets do not meet the criteria, then distribution would create a requisition.
Requestions is the act of establishing a demand or signaling a requirement.
Requestioning by its nature can come in two forms:
a. Forecast signal. Where the data of consumption rate, capacity, and order/ship time
determines the availability of current inventory compared to future demand can
generate a requisition.
b. Customer direct demand. Where the customer has a direct need beyond what is
currently available, forecasted, and/or determined and is usually based on unique
mission parameters or circumstances previously unforeseeable.
In terms of routing, the routing of the requisition depends on the authority to release
assets according to the next level’s distribution cycle.
3.5.2.3 Fulfillment: Make or Buy
The action of satisfying the demand leads to the decision to make or buy. These two actions fall
under two sub-factors agnostic of echelon, scale, and/or scope:
a. Make (internally source): The decision to make is based on the data indicating if it is
more effective, efficient, or supportive to make, internally source, or provide the
service, information, and/or materiel to satisfy what is requested. This will lead to a
production action.
b. Buy (externally source): The decision to buy means to purchase/obtain a service,
information, and or materiel outside the organization. This will lead to a procurement
action, usually attributed to materiel, but can apply to services and information.

40

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
3.5.2.4 Storage
Storage operations is the act of receiving, holding, protecting, and preparing something until it is
to be issued, assigned, or dispensed. While this sub-function pertains mainly to materiel, it
certainly has immediate application to both services and information. Storage, as a cyclic
function, is comprised of four subordinate functions:
1) Receipting. The act of accepting inventory into the activity to make it available. The
action also provides an audit trail of where the inventory originated, who accepted it into
the inventory, when it entered the inventory, and where it is being kept.
2) Inventory Control. Inventory control ensures the accurate accountability of assets
between what is the real-world physical quantity and the recorded data medium.
Additionally, it ensures the inventory is moved to the assigning, dispensing, and issuing
function only by authorized individuals acting under the appropriate authority.
3) Care-in-Storage: This subfunction is primarily concerned with the appropriate rotation of
stock, ensures serviceability, and preserves usability.
4) Safekeeping: The protection from the elements, theft/pilferage, and in some cases
limiting observation/accessibility (as a force protection measure) are all part of the
physical safekeeping of inventory.
3.5.2.5 Disposal and Salvage
The end of the lifecycle or usefulness of inventory culminates in either disposal or salvage.
Supply is rarely the physical executor of disposal or salvage operations, rather it is the
administrative provider reporting the action. The result of either action relieves the government
from the responsibilities of accounting and maintaining the inventory.
1) Disposal is the act of removing the inventory physically and administratively from active
use, availability, and accessibility according to its original function. Disposal consists of
three subordinate functional methods:
a. Transfer. The physical and administrative movement of the inventory to another
Department of Defense (DoD) activity.
b. Sale. The exchange of money/funds, when authorized, to civilians; foreign
governments, and/or departments/agencies outside of DoD.
c. Abandonment. Leaving the inventory, usually on the field of battle, when recovery or
other methods of disposal are not possible or viable. This method is the least
desirable of all other disposal methods as it leaves the possibility of salvage or
reclamation to adversarial forces. Every effort should be made to ensure the
inventory is totally disabled and proves no value in terms of functional use and/or
reverse engineering.
2) Salvage is the act of separating the components of the asset into usable inventory
outside of its original function. Salvage consists of three subordinate functional methods:
a. Reuse. To use the inventory for another purpose other than it was originally
intended. Usually converting the inventory to a garrison or administrative role rather
than its original combat/war-time purpose. This action requires the facilitation of
accountability from combat inventory roles to garrison/administrative inventory roles.
b. Recycle. Breaking down the original inventory into its useful subcomponents that can
be used in other like items or satisfy open demands for such subcomponents.

41

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
c. Scrap. Reducing the inventory into its primary elemental components as raw
material.

3.5.3 Data Exchange Requirements
The data exchange requirements presented here remove the customer’s burden of being
acquainted with the complexities in supply requisitioning procedures. Additionally, this
methodology streamlines requests into a single form where any class of supply (and sub-class)
can be requested. Therefore, anything from paper, pens, MREs, ammunition, bulk fuel, medical
supplies, etc. are all ordered on this form and would be separated out to the different
departments for fulfilment. These separate departments would then add any necessary
additional data, a process extraneous to the requisitioner.
1) Segment Number. System assigned, as the request is created under the header. A
single digit indicator; up to nine independent request types can be added under the
header but does not restrict the number of individual lines contained in the request.
2) Request Type. Expressed as a two-digit background code when the user selects “Supply
Request”. In this case, SR is the code for a Supply Request.
3) Request Priority. Expressed as two-digit code based on the urgency aligned to the
element/unit’s F/AD. User selects from a clear text list of Critical, Urgent, or Routine.
4) Item by NIIN. When the user selects the clear text name of the item(s) to be requested,
the system will populate the NIIN into the request segment. The user is not expected to
know the NIIN but could select an item based on the NIIN. Selection lists can be
preloaded with the most common items regardless of Class. The supply request is
agnostic to classifications; therefore, ammunition, meal ready to eat (MRE), fuel, etc. are
requested on a singular form and is parsed out at the supporting activity for action by
commodity group. If the NIIN or item name is unknown, the user can input a description
in the “Narrative” box. Supporting activity can update the NIIN field once technical
research is complete in identifying the materiel item(s) required.
5) Item Quantity. Expressed as a five-digit numeric quantity. User inputs the quantity
desired. Unit of issue (U/I) or Unit of Measure (UOM) are background enterprise data
elements which does not need to be passed. These terms should be displayed but the
data is irrelevant to the data exchange requirement.
6) Required Delivery Date. Local. Expressed as local date CCYYMMMDD assuming
midnight to 23:59 as a full day. User inputs desired date the supplies are to be delivered.
7) Delivery Location. Geospatial reference expressed in Military Grid Reference System
(MGRS). Position 1-2: Alpha (Grid Zone Designator, GZD), Position 3-4: Alpha (100Kmeter Square Identifier), Position 5-14: Numeric (Numerical Location) depending on
accuracy level within 1-, 10-, and 100-meter intervals: position 5-9: Numeric (Easting)
and position 10-14: Numeric (Northing). Remarks: Physical locations can be pre-loaded
as a predetermined clear text descriptor (i.e., White Beach, Pier 3, etc.) for ease of use.
8) Attachment. Expressed as an embedded object of a video, picture, or voice recording of
the of the item(s) being requested. Flag indicator if there is an attachment with a single
digit code (1: Yes, 0: N).
9) Narrative. Free text for additional information up to 100 characters to describe the
materiel demand when a NIIN does not exist (i.e., bulk water) or additional coordination.

3.6 Maintenance Data Requirements
Maintenance is active restoration of equipment to functionally perform its combat mission (i.e.,
shoot, move, and/or communicate). Even with the application of predictive analytics and
condition-based monitoring, the maintenance mission is primarily reactive to the outputs of the
equipment’s operating efficiency. Within the context of the operating environment, conditionbased monitoring and predictive maintenance may be severely limited due to the availability of
communication pathways. Therefore, the human owner/operator of military equipment is the first
and most essential maintenance “sensor” in the logistics kill chain to report and identify
equipment maintenance issues. Requesting maintenance will remain a crucial component for
ensuring operational availability to support of mission execution. It should be noted the
maintenance function is not responsible for materiel “readiness reporting” but the actions of
maintenance produces ‘readiness’ as a byproduct. However, readiness should not be the litmus
test for maintenance execution and efficiency.

Figure 16: Maintenance Function Cycle

43

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004

3.6.1 Functional Interdependencies
3.6.1.1 Supply Function
The ability for maintenance to execute its mission relies on two areas in the supply:
1) The ability to procure materiel requests in support of the maintenance function,
specifically in Class III and IX.
2) The ability to request or confirm unrepairable government equipment for further
disposition in terms of disposal or salvage.
3.6.1.2 Mobility Function
Maintenance, and the ability to recover or free from immobility, requires the service provider
execute tasking, dispatching, and capacity planning sub-functions of mobility. Functionally, this
can be performed at the appropriate echelon regarding scale/scope, but it does necessitate the
requirement for access to the required data to perform.

3.6.2 Processing Cycle at the Functional Level
3.6.2.1 Recovery
1) Retrieve. The asset is not able to move or operate on its own power and must be
brought back to a predetermined location for follow on maintenance classification. This
type of recovery does not necessarily apply only to self-propelled assets but can apply to
any military equipment.
2) Free from Immobility (Unstuck). Once the asset is free from the condition of immobility, it
is able to operate under its own power.
3.6.2.2 Inspection
When a defect or maintenance issue is suspected or identified, the maintenance activity will
check and/or test the condition before entering the maintenance cycle. The check/test is not
diagnostics but confirmation of the condition which warrants entry into the maintenance cycle.
1) Check. A check is usually performed in response to fault indicator, notification, or sensor
reading or some other condition monitoring method. As automated notifications rarely
indicate the actual cause, a mechanic/technician must investigate the area/place/system
of fault to confirm the condition.
2) Test. Usually done in response to a condition found by the owner/operator. The
mechanic/technician recreates or exposes the defect through a standardized process—
or through a similar condition the fault was discovered—in order to confirm maintenance
is required.
3.6.2.3 Classification: Unrepairable Conditions
Unrepairable conditions are governed by cost, obsolesce, service life, and/or maintenance
limitations. The maintenance activity could classify an asset based on any one or combination of
these conditions. Based on the identified conditions, the maintenance activity will request
disposition instructions from higher headquarters. Typically, requests for disposition will be
routed to the appropriate program manager, who has final decisional authority and will provide
instruction for follow-on action.

44

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
3.6.2.4 Classification: Repairable Conditions
Repairable conditions are deemed to be within the capacity, scope, authority, cost, skill, and
capabilities of the maintenance activity either organically or by next higher maintenance activity.
Repairable conditions will bring the asset to its combat functional use. The repairable conditions
are in the following categories:
1) Modifications. An authorized change in the original asset’s form, fit, or function. The
management of modifications of an asset is not a maintenance activity responsibility.
However, the application/installation of the modification is a maintenance function.
Owners/operators of assets requiring modifications will request maintenance support for
directed modifications per the modification instruction.
2) Servicing. Cyclic and routine preventative maintenance (usually annual) outside
owner/operator responsibility identified in the asset’s technical manual. Minor
adjustments and tuning which an owner/operator would not have the tools, facilities,
training, and/or authority to perform are a servicing action conducted by the maintenance
function.
3) Calibration. Periodic adjustments for precision equipment/tools. Calibration requires
specialized tools, testing equipment, and training to ensure the established operating
standards are met. Owner/operators of precision equipment/tools request calibration to
the supporting maintenance activity at the appropriate interval per the technical manual.
4) Repair. Most commonly referred to as ‘corrective maintenance’. This action brings an
asset from a degraded or deadline condition to operational condition focusing a specific
defect or set of defects. If the defect is repairable but out of the current maintenance
activity’s capability to perform, the asset will be evacuated to the next higher
maintenance activity. Within the repair action, a subset of more in-depth repair can occur
but is usually performed at the depot level maintenance activity:
a. Overhaul. The restoration of an asset’s systems and/or major components. An
overhaul is primarily concerned and focused on the replacing or restoring of primary
components.
b. Rebuild. The systematic disassembly on an asset down to its core/basic components
and then reconstructed to a new or near new like condition. Every element of the
asset from internal wiring to paint is replaced and/or repaired.
c. Reclamation. Both overhaul and rebuild result in reclamation. Reclamation is to
repair an asset and return it to the supply inventory system in usable condition.
Reclamation is rarely conducted by organizational maintenance activities and only by
direct authorization from a higher-level authority.

3.6.3 Data Exchange Requirements
The following data entries are constructed around the precept the requestor is not a
mechanic/technician by specialty but more likely is the owner/operator of the equipment at the
lowest tactical level. It also must be assumed the owner/operator has exhausted all available
maintenance tasks authorized at their level. Lastly, it must be assumed additional data needed
by the mechanic/technician is federated and available (i.e., maintenance history,
predictive/condition monitoring, etc.). Therefore, the maintenance request is not centered
around ‘parts ordering’ but maintenance as a service which may conclude or result in parts
being ordered. The form can also be used to evacuate equipment from one maintenance activity
to another. The intent is for data to create the maintenance record once synchronized with the

45

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
enterprise logistics IT system and serves as the beginning of an ongoing maintenance
conversation.
1) Segment Number. System assigned as the request is created under the header. A single
digit indicator: up to nine independent request types can be added under the header.
2) Request Type. Expressed as a two-digit background code when the user selects
“Maintenance Request”. In this case CM is the code for data exchange.
3) Request Priority. Expressed as two-digit code based on the urgency aligned to the
element/unit’s F/AD. Based on the “Equipment Operational Condition” Routing Operational: A, Routine - Minor (can perform most combat functions); B, Urgent Degraded (can perform some combat functions; C, Critical - Deadline (cannot perform
any combat functions – cannot shoot, move, or communicate).
4) Serial Number. Optional if not serialized. User selects from available selection within
their data slice or EDL. The serial number acts as a header for report data. This data
segment is optional since not all reparable equipment is serialized. If the item is not
serialized or found in the data slice, then the NIIN and/or name of item for repair must be
added. If the serial number is longer than 15 digits, only the last 15 digits (to include
special characters) will be transmitted.
5) National Item Identification Number (NIIN). Auto populated if the serial number is
selected. Expressed as a 9-position alpha-numeric code (XXXXXXXXX).
6) Model of Equipment. Auto populated based on the NIIN and/or serial number. If neither
are selected the user can activate the text box to input the common model number up to
20 characters. If the serial number is selected, this information is not passed.
7) Item Nomenclature. Auto populated based on the NIIN, serial number, and/or model
number. If neither are selected the user can activate the text box to input the common
item nomenclature up to 20 characters. If the serial number or NIIN is selected, this
information is not passed.
8) Number of Pieces Requiring Maintenance Support. Auto calculated based on the
number of serial numbers inputted. If the equipment is not serialized the user can input
in the text box for the numeric number of items up three digits (XXX).
9) Equipment Operational Condition. User determines based on their operational need and
mission parameters if the equipment is combat ready/mission capable. User selects the
clear text operational condition which will be populated with the corresponding singledigit alpha code for data exchange. Operational (can perform all combat functions): A,
Minor (can perform most combat functions); B, Degraded (can perform some combat
functions; C, Deadline (cannot perform any combat functions – i.e., cannot shoot, move
or communicate).
10) Date Maintenance Support is Required. Expressed as local date CCYYMMMDD
assuming midnight to 23:59 as a full day.
11) Location of Equipment. The physical location the equipment will be or is currently
located. Geospatial reference expressed in Military Grid Reference System (MGRS).
Position 1-2: Alpha (Grid Zone Designator, GZD), Position 3-4: Alpha (100K-meter
Square Identifier), Position 5-14: Numeric (Numerical Location) depending on accuracy
level within 1-, 10-, and 100-meter intervals: position 5-9: Numeric (Easting) and position
10-14: Numeric (Northing). Remarks: Physical locations can be pre-loaded as a
predetermined clear text descriptor (i.e., White Beach, Pier 3, etc.) for ease of use.

46

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
12) Type of Maintenance Support Required. User selects the clear text maintenance support
requested which will be populated with the corresponding two-digit alpha numeric code
for data exchange. Repair: R1, Recovery – Retrieve: R2, Recovery – Free from
Immobility: R3, Contact Team: R4. Defaults to None: XX
13) Type of Repair. User selects the clear text maintenance repair type requested which will
be populated with the corresponding two-digit alpha numeric code for data exchange.
This list activates only when the type of maintenance support required is “Repair – R1.”
Modification: M1, Servicing - PM: S1, Servicing - Tune/Adjust: S2, Calibration: C1,
Repair Defect: D1.
14) Repair Major Defect. List activates when the type of maintenance support required is
“Repair: R1” or “Contact Team: R4” only. Based on the item serial number or NIIN the
list of available option will dynamically change. If the item is vehicular in nature, then
only defects unique to the vehicle will be selectable with other defects becoming hidden
(i.e., “transmission/output circuitry” would not be selectable as this pertains to electronics
not vehicles). User selects the “best fit” clear text maintenance repair major defect which
will be populated with the corresponding four-digit alpha numeric code for data
exchange.
a. MD01 - Engine: Issues with the starter, alternator, belts, rough start, or anything in
this area. Can also be applied to generators, heavy equipment, and water/fuel
pumping systems.
b. MD02 - Fuel: Issues with fuel systems, carburation, fuel mixture, fuel leaks, or
anything pertaining to the onboard storage, filtering, fuel quality, or internal
movement of the fuel.
c. MD03 - Exhaust: Issues with the output burned gasses routing, color, rhythm, or
smell.
d. MD04 - Cooling: Issues with retaining adequate levels, leaks, overheating, or running
cold.
e. MD05 - Lubrication: Issues with engine, transmission, or other internal friction
components’ ability to internally lubricate due to leaking, low levels, etc.
f.

MD06 - Electrical/Fuse: Issues with the onboard electrical systems to maintain
system voltage, operate lights or connected components, and/or fuses/cables
overheating/tripping/short circuiting.

g. MD07 - Transmission/Power Train: Issues where the equipment does not efficiently
transfer engine generated power to the system and/or does not operate at expected
parameters under load.
h. MD08 - Chassis/Body/Frame/Armor: Issues with the structural mounting of the
system and/or ballistic protective layer(s).
i.

MD09 - Suspension/Axle/Differential/Shocks: Issues with the ability of the equipment
to absorb ground impacts and/or maintain ground clearance and/or transfer power
train power efficiently through the axle(s).

j.

MD10 - Brakes/Tires/Wheels/Hub/Track: Issues with tire inflation, internal run flat
systems, central tire inflation system, tire spindles, mounting hubs, calibers, track,
track shoes, and braking system.

47

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
k. MD11 - Lift/Winch/Boom/Turret: Issues with mounted special purpose equipment
pertaining to the ability to lift, pull, rotate, etc.
l.

MD12 - Hydraulics: Issues with the internal system to operate hydraulically controlled
systems due to low pressure, leaks, maintaining fluid levels, and/or
electromechanical control of hydraulically controlled systems.

m. MD13 - Receiver/Input circuitry: Issues with the ability to receive or input data from
external sources.
n. MD14 - Transmitter/Output circuitry: Issues with the ability to transfer or output data
to external sources.
o. MD15 - Display/Screen/Video: Issues with the graphical user interfaces or displays
required to operate equipment.
p. MD16 - Weapon System: Issues with the ability operate, load, unload, aim, target, or
execute firing of a weapon system (projectile, missile, and/or munition based).
q. NMAJ - Routine/No Major Defect: Used for required preventive maintenance and/or
minor/degrading issues.
15) Attachment. Expressed as an embedded object of a video, picture, or voice recording of
the maintenance condition. Flag indicator if there is an attachment with a single digit
code (1: Yes, 0: N).
16) Narrative. Free text for additional information up to 100 characters.

3.7 General Engineering Data Requirements
Unlike the other logistics functions, General Engineering does not have a distinct functional
cycle. This function provides both critical services and materiel, however, it is the least
supported function by information technology. Every service provided by this function involves
collecting, recording, transcribing, and reporting service data in a pre-formatted, analog, paper-

48

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
based form which is NOT converted or transliterated into a logistics information system of any
kind. Engineering data is therefore not readily accessible in digital environments.
Notable changes within this function:
1) Bulk water and bulk fuel are combined into a single sub-function of ‘Bulk Liquids’ as the
production, storage, and dispensing are all similar functions.
2) Assault Bridging and other standard bridging systems are no longer organic capabilities
of the Marine Corps and are therefore omitted from this analysis. However, Bridging
remains a doctrinal sub-function of General Engineering.
3) Power Generation and Power Distribution is an operational/strategic-level planning
event. The tactical user at the lowest echelon would not request services with regards to
generating or distributing power and is reserved for a higher-level data planning.
4) Explosive Ordnance Disposal (EOD) functions as Support Forces, Supporting
Establishment, Homeland Defense, and Special Operations Forces, by locating,
accessing, identifying, rendering safe, neutralizing, and disposing of hazards from
foreign and domestic Chemical Biological Radiological Nuclear and High-Yield
Explosives (CBRNE), Unexploded Explosive Ordnance (UXO), Improvised Explosive
Devices (IED), and Weapons of Mass Destruction (WMD) that present a threat to
operations, installations, personnel, or materiel. These basic data exchange
requirements are presented here for tactical users to request EOD support.
5) Facilities maintenance is an operational/strategic-level planned event, as required.
Tactical users would not request this service, although they would identify significant
landmarks for planning considerations at higher echelons.
6) Vertical construction is an operational/strategic-level event used to strengthen and
rehabilitate existing infrastructure. Vertical infrastructure is a sustainment consideration
and consumes human and mechanical resources.
7) Horizontal construction includes the excavation and construction of roads, railways,
bridges, cantonment sites, and gap crossings. Construction at this level is an
operational/strategic-level planning event as it involves the construction of airfields (not
repair or utilization of existing airfields), constructing supply routes or improving existing
routes (to include repair, but not tactical movement repair), ordnance structures that
include bunkers of various explosive weights for long storage (not an expeditious/field
ammo storage point), and site prep/bed down that includes hardened structures, that
can use existing facilities for rehabilitation when requested as a vertical construction
requirement.
8) Enemy munitions/ordnance exploitation is dependent on the planning outputs of the
Marine Corps Planning Process (MCPP). These planning considerations are not
pertinent to the tactical user. However, organizational planning would include the
reporting of enemy munitions, ammunition, and ordnance—typically retrieved from
information contained within the SALUTE report. Decisions for exploitation are made at
the operational/strategic-level. There are occasions when data is required to “exploit”
known enemy infrastructure, positions, and/or equipment/supplies. These types of
reports are already contained within the Area and Zone Reconnaissance Reports with
the option of “Attachment/Media Object” and “Narrative” to support exploitation
decisions.

49

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004

3.7.1 Functional Interdependencies
3.7.1.1 Mobility
Initially, when movement is required beyond the engineer platoon’s organic capability, a
movement request is generated. When the request is generated, it is either as a ground, littoral,
or aerial or a combination of any of those modes. Follow-on requests will be made once the
reconnaissance report has been submitted and can be fulfilled in any manner.
Engineer equipment, especially heavy equipment, requires mobility to move from home location
to destination, or from home location to port of embarkation.
3.7.1.2 Supply
Supply procurement is generated from requirements for multiple shipping locations and
variations in classes of supply. Each request for service can contain like-matter items such as
Class III and IV or be singular in nature such as Class V. Often, the requirement for
procurement comes from locally-sourced materials and require a contracting officer.
It is important to consider the possibility that a “make” or “buy” decision is needed as an
interdependent requirement for maintenance services or in the performance of maintenance
activities.
3.7.1.3 Maintenance
Organizational repair is done organically, and, if needed, a request for evacuation of the item
will be made. A request for higher echelon maintenance does not mean the equipment in need
should or will be evacuated; it may simply need additional maintenance expertise that is not
organic to the local engineer team.
There are occasions when an item of equipment cannot be repaired organically and must be
evacuated back to the organizational-level, or, in most cases, intermediate-level maintenance
facility. When this occurs, additional equipment is required to continue the operation.
Forecasting at this point is vital for the mission to continue seamlessly.

3.7.2 Processing Cycle at the Functional Level
General Engineering does not have a functional-level cycle of events that takes place for a
product or service. There are services that general engineering provides that are part of the
planning; however, the resulting service output products do not exist in any digital realm or
system.

3.7.3 Data Exchange Requirements
During the analysis it was noted that much of the data contained within Engineer
Reconnaissance reports were duplicate entries and used outdated doctrine as a standard.
When an engineer reconnaissance team (ERT) conducts a survey, media (i.e., images,
geospatial tags, and remarks) is typically added to a journal entry. Equally, when estimations
are performed for requesting or supplying bulk liquids, antiquated and inaccurate forecasting
techniques are applied. There exists no official digital reference for accurately forecasting
platform-specific bulk liquid consumption rates.
While the tactical user may not have specifically requested general engineering services (i.e.,
route reconnaissance), these services are essential in the performance the unit’s assigned
mission. As such, there exists a fundamental data interdependency on other functional areas.
The data captured from the ERT should reflect the same geospatial area as a point of reference
and seamlessly transfer to other teams and users to review and append as necessary.

50

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
Likewise, if an additional ERT is subsequently employed, real-time geospatial atmospherics
should be captured to enable modification to estimations and mobility requirements.
At present, ERTs manually produce “sketches” using stand-alone, disconnected handheld
electronic measuring devices. To enable “digital truth”, application tools that allow the user to
remain covered/concealed must be incorporated into the information system, without the need
to manually convert scale/distance of objects. Automating this process will greatly reduce—if not
eliminate—the potential for quantitative human error factors when these calculations are
conducted manually. Providing digital pictures, videos, and voice recorded descriptions are now
an essential data requirement to provide precise information on areas, roads, and routes.
Once this data becomes available in the digital environment, recommendations for missionspecific EDLs may be automated.
3.7.3.1 Engineer Reconnaissance – Area Report
This is a general report to detail information concerning threat, terrain, society, and
infrastructure of an area a unit must move through or occupy.
1) Date of Evaluation/Reconnaissance. Expressed as local date CCYYMMMDD assuming
midnight to 23:59 as a full day.
2) Element Leader. References the reconnaissance/engineer leader performing the
reported data. Expressed as either 8 to 9-digit ZAP or 10-digit EDI-PI. ZAP data element
is a preformatted and system assigned abbreviation for USMC personnel. EDI-PI is DoD
assigned for all DoD personnel (active, reserve, GS, and contractor). User should not
necessarily need to type in these identifiers but select from a preloaded list on the local
machine or scan a government DoD issued identification card if the individual is not in
the list.
3) Area Location. Used to indicate the general area of the reconnaissance. Geospatial
reference expressed in Military Grid Reference System (MGRS). Position 1-2: Alpha
(Grid Zone Designator, GZD), Position 3-4: Alpha (100K-meter Square Identifier),
Position 5-14: Numeric (Numerical Location) depending on accuracy level within 1-, 10-,
and 100-meter intervals: position 5-9: Numeric (Easting) and position 10-14: Numeric
(Northing). Remarks: Physical can be pre-loaded as a predetermined clear text
descriptor (i.e., White Beach, Pier 3, etc.) for ease of use.
4) Items Report. General Engineering report embedded within the overall item instance of
the mission report. This report consists of individual references of interest. Expressed as
a 14 position MGRS locator and free text block for type/label up to 50 text characters.
5) Water Source. If free-flowing water features are found within the area, a report of the
flow rate/velocity expressed as a fixed four position numeric value. Quantity (kiloliters)
fixed 4 position numeric value.
6) Attachment/Media objects. Expressed as an embedded video, picture, or voice-recorded
file at the time of request. Flag indicator if there is an attachment with a single digit code
(1: Yes, 0: N).
7) Narrative. Free text for additional information up to 100 characters in length to describe
the area in general not contained in the items report.

3.7.3.2 Engineer Reconnaissance – Zone Report
A zone reconnaissance is assigned when the threat situation is vague or when information
concerning cross-country trafficability is desired.
1) Date of Evaluation/Reconnaissance. Expressed as local date CCYYMMMDD assuming
midnight to 23:59 as a full day.
2) Element Leader. Expressed as either 8 to 9-digit ZAP or 10-digit EDI-PI. ZAP data
element is a preformatted and system assigned abbreviation for USMC personnel. EDIPI is DoD assigned for all DoD personnel (active, reserve, GS, and contractor). User
should not necessarily need to type in these identifiers but select from a preloaded list
on the local machine or scan a government DoD issued identification card if the
individual is not in the list.
3) Amphibious Crossing Classification. If a water feature can be crossed. This is not the
same as a ford report as this is mainly used for amphibious vehicles (i.e., AAV/ACV,
LAV, LCAC, etc.). Expressed in alpha numeric context in three data blocks, for example
“EWHITE400” (Type = E; Classification=WHITE; Frontage in Meters=440)
4) Enemy Report. This report consists of individual references to enemy positions,
emplacements, facilities, supplies, and/or equipment. 14 position MGRS locator and free
text block for type/label up to 50 text characters.
5) Zone Location. Used to indicate the zone of the reconnaissance. Geospatial reference
expressed in Military Grid Reference System (MGRS). Position 1-2: Alpha (Grid Zone
Designator, GZD), Position 3-4: Alpha (100K-meter Square Identifier), Position 5-14:
Numeric (Numerical Location) depending on accuracy level within 1-, 10-, and 100-meter
intervals: position 5-9: Numeric (Easting) and position 10-14: Numeric (Northing).
Attached media/object/free is needed for specific approach/cover and
concealment/obstacles/Observations.
6) Water Source. If free flowing water features are found within the area a report of the flow
rate/velocity expressed as a fixed four position numeric value. Quantity (kiloliters) fixed 4
position numeric value.
7) Items Report. General Engineering report embedded within the overall item instance of
the mission report. This report consists of individual references of interest. 14 position
MGRS locator and free text block for type/label up to 50 text characters.
8) Attachment/Media Objects. Expressed as an embedded object of a video, picture, or
voice recording of the of the item(s) being requested. Flag indicator if there is an
attachment with a single digit code (1: Yes, 0: N).

52

FA8075-18-D-0004
9) Narrative. Free text for additional information up to 100 characters to describe the zone
not presented in the data.

3.7.3.3 Engineer Reconnaissance – Route Report
Route reconnaissance is the directed effort to obtain information (focused on the commanders’
reconnaissance objective(s)) along a specified route and on adjacent terrain (including lateral
routes) over which movement may be restricted.
1) Date of Evaluation/Reconnaissance. Expressed as local date CCYYMMMDD assuming
midnight to 23:59 as a full day.
2) Element Leader. Expressed as either 8 to 9-digit ZAP or 10-digit EDI-PI. ZAP data
element is a preformatted and system assigned abbreviation for USMC personnel. EDIPI is DoD assigned for all DoD personnel (active, reserve, government service [GS], and
contractor). User should not necessarily need to type in these identifiers but select from
a preloaded list on the local machine or scan a government DoD issued identification
card if the individual is not in the list.
3) Enemy Report. This report consists of individual references to enemy positions,
emplacements, facilities, supplies, and/or equipment. 14 position MGRS locator and free
text block for type/label up to 50 text characters or by drop down list.
4) Route Information. Geospatial reference expressed in Military Grid Reference System
(MGRS). Position 1-2: Alpha (Grid Zone Designator, GZD), Position 3-4: Alpha (100Kmeter Square Identifier), Position 5-14: Numeric (Numerical Location) depending on
accuracy level within 1-, 10-, and 100-meter intervals: position 5-9: Numeric (Easting)
and position 10-14: Numeric (Northing). Attached media/object/free is needed for
specific approach/cover and concealment/obstacles/observations. May use ‘way points’
to determine the route reviewed.
5) Water Source. If free flowing water features are found within the area a report of the flow
rate/velocity expressed as a fixed four position numeric value. Quantity (kiloliters) fixed 4
position numeric value.
6) Items Report. General Engineering report embedded within the overall item instance of
the mission report. This report consists of individual references of interest. 14 position
MGRS locator and free text block for type/label up to 50 text characters.
7) Attachment/Media Objects. Expressed as an embedded object of a video, picture, or
voice recording of the of the item(s) being requested. Flag indicator if there is an
attachment with a single digit code (1: Yes, 0: N).

53

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
8) Narrative. Free text for additional information up to 100 characters to describe the route
not revealed in the data elements.

3.7.3.4 Engineer Reconnaissance – Road Report
The road report evaluates the condition and trafficability of established roads. The road report is
different from a route report as it focuses only on the road conditions along a predetermined
start/end point and does not include cross-country routes. If a road encounters a tunnel or
bridge, a separate report is submitted for each instance of such features.
1) Date of Evaluation/Reconnaissance. Expressed as local date CCYYMMMDD assuming
midnight to 23:59 as a full day.
2) Start Point Location. Geospatial reference expressed in Military Grid Reference System
(MGRS). Position 1-2: Alpha (Grid Zone Designator, GZD), Position 3-4: Alpha (100Kmeter Square Identifier), Position 5-14: Numeric (Numerical Location) depending on
accuracy level within 1-, 10-, and 100-meter intervals: position 5-9: Numeric (Easting)
and position 10-14: Numeric (Northing). Remarks: Physical can be pre-loaded as a
predetermined clear text descriptor (i.e., White Beach, Pier 3, etc.) for ease of use.
3) End Point Location. Geospatial reference expressed in Military Grid Reference System
(MGRS). Position 1-2: Alpha (Grid Zone Designator, GZD), Position 3-4: Alpha (100Kmeter Square Identifier), Position 5-14: Numeric (Numerical Location) depending on
accuracy level within 1-, 10-, and 100-meter intervals: position 5-9: Numeric (Easting)
and position 10-14: Numeric (Northing). Remarks: Physical can be pre-loaded as a
predetermined clear text descriptor (i.e., White Beach, Pier 3, etc.) for ease of use.
4) Road Classification. Expressed as a single digit code when the user selects the clear
text option A: Flat Gradients/ Easy Curves, B: Steep Gradient (>7%), C: Sharp Curves
(Less than 30M), D: Steep Gradients and Sharp Curves
5) Drainage. Expressed as a single digit code when the user selects the clear text option A:
Adequate, Crown/Camber with adequate culverts in good condition, B: Inadequate, Poor
crown/ Camber, culverts or ditches are blocked or otherwise in poor condition.
6) Foundation. Expressed as a single digit code when the user selects the clear text option
A: Stabilized and compacted. B: Unstable, loose
7) Surface Type. Expressed as a single digit code when the user selects the clear text
option A: Concrete, B: Bituminous, C: Brick, D: Stone, E: Crushed Rock, F: Water bound
Macadum, G: Gravel, H: Lightly Metaled, I: Natural or Stabilized Soil, J: Other (activates
text box for 50 characters)

54

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
8) Obstructions. A: Serial Number Assigned, B: Particulars (Obstacles, Utilities, Power
lines, Intersections), C: Grid Reference, D: Remarks
9) Attachment. Expressed as an embedded object of a video, picture, or voice recording of
the maintenance condition. Flag indicator if there is an attachment with a single digit
code (1: Yes, 0: N).
10) Narrative. Free text for additional information up to 100 characters to describe the
materiel demand or additional coordination.
