Section 4: Critical Capability Gaps and Recommended Doctrinal Changes
4.1 Digital Link Between Operational Planning and Logistics Gap
4.1.1 Problem Overview
As logistics is the pacing function for operations, the analysis has identified a critical capability
gap in the warfighting function’s data flow, organization, and data development/synthesis
process to support the commander’s command and control efforts. This gap is specifically and
especially resident in the realm of operational logistics (OPLOG). Through the analysis of data
exchange requirements, it was observed that operational requirements do not align well to
logistical capabilities or were completely devoid of operational cohesion or assessment within
the realm of logistics competencies.
The link between Logistics with Operations and Intelligence to provide true command & control
awareness and capability to the Combatant Commander/Joint Force Commander is inherently
flawed, inefficient, or not fully understood, negatively impacting a forces ability to
forecast/anticipate future logistical requirements to meet the operational demand. The result is
the commander exercising and making command and control decisions based on assessments
which may not be fully informed despite best efforts, creating a commander’s decision paradox.
Therefore, the analysis team identified a key exasperating condition where planners are
developing courses of action within the confines of PowerPoints, whiteboards, and analog
methodologies without the ability to connect digitally to logistics. Planners are constrained in
relating and considering the relevance of after-action reports, expenditures, and lessons learned
due to accessibility. Due to accessibility, the systematic and consistent consideration of
enterprise-wide historical information is rarely deliberated in the planning process of similar
operations. Due to the analog nature and the missing component of the lessons of the past,
logistics is not fully aware of the requirements being formed and conversely planners are
making assumptions on the logistics capabilities they are applying requirements on which may
not be in the boundaries of logistical reach.
As such, planners currently do not have a common enterprise-wide mission planning and
support tool that provides/displays a Common Operational Picture (COP) that efficiently
interfaces, informs, and digitally feeds logistics planning, forecasting, and execution information
technology tools. Additionally, key operational data on unit locations, callsigns, and other
pertinent information directly impacting the ability to logistically sustain operations is not
contained in any logistics information system and is not directly tied to an existing COP. C2PC
is inefficient and ineffective in providing/capturing mission requirements, historical data, task
organizing, and associated reports (POA&M, TEEP, FRAGO, WARNO, OPORD, Cmdr
Guidance, mission reports, etc.) as expected or required by the Marine Corps Planning Process
(MCPP).

87

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004

Figure 18: Marine Corps Planning Process

4.1.2 Impacts to Command and Control
Mission parameters/objectives and/or commander’s intent establish within the functions the
types of variables that need study to support the command-and-control decision. The staff then
collect data from the appropriate sources, organizes the data, visualizes the data, and then
analyzes the collected data to reach a set of conclusions, courses of actions, and options.

Figure 19: Command and Control (C2)

The best conclusions, courses of actions, and options are those that are realistic, manageable,
and concise which paint the picture for the commander in realism, is manageable, and precise
with an understanding of the influences on the other functions as to what is required from the
interdependencies. All of these aspects culminate in the commander’s decision advantage.
Decision advantage is the ability of the commander to make clear strategically sound decisions
faster than the competitor or adversary. As such, the current conditions of C2 does not lend
itself to decision advantage especially where logistics is concerned.

4.1.3 Impacts to Operational Reach
Understanding operational reach is bound by the capabilities of logistics to provide what is
required and where logistics is bound by time to provide the requirement. The function of
logistics seeks to extend the boundaries of the operation. Hence, logistics is the pacing function
for operations.
Operations is the focus of the Marine Corps specifically, its ability to allow Marine Corps forces
to seamlessly deploy, project power, and fight from naval vessels or austere expeditionary
bases, or any combination thereof.
The enabling aspect of operations is logistics. Logistics enables operational reach. Operational
reach is the requirements to achieve an objective.

88

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004

Figure 20: Operational Boundaries (1 of 3)

Operational reach boundaries are dictated by the ability of logistics (i.e., you can’t go an extra
mile on an empty tank of gas). Therefore, operations are bounded by logistics and can only
operate within that boundary.

Figure 21: Operational Boundaries (2 of 3)

Even so, logistics itself is not boundless. Logistics boundaries are dictated by time and distance
(space) which can or cannot enable speed, agility, and resilience in the context of the
operational goal.

Figure 22: Operational Boundaries (3 of 3)

Operations are influenced by the ability of logistics to function effectively and efficiently within
the parameters of time and distance. Logistics information systems are not adequately enabling
the functions of logistics to overcome and anticipate operational requirements. In many cases
the Marines are working for the system vice the system working for the Marines.

4.1.4 Operations Disconnect from Logistics
Planners are executing MCPP and planning functions manually and transferring these manual
functions into analog nonstandard formats of PowerPoints, and Word Documents. Logisticians
extract pertinent operational information from these formats into intermediatory logistics
planning tools usually in Excel Spreadsheets. Once logistics planning is complete, this
information is transferred into the various logistics systems for execution.

Figure 23: Operational Informational Flow

Through this process, the data becomes more fragmented and disassociated from its
origination. Additionally, the manual nature of planning does not lend a direct correlation to
lessons learned or informs the supporting logistics functions. Historical planning documents are
archived in a variety of methods which are not standard throughout the enterprise.

89

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004

4.1.5 Desired End State
A planner’s workbench enables posting of a current, future, and historical operations in a single
application, informing logistics planners through a common operating picture that is viewed
jointly amongst operations, logistics, and intelligence communities on a cross domain solution
(SIPR, NIPR, and CENTRIX).
Consistent with the OPLOG model proposal, implement an operations/log planning tool with the
ability to:
1) Create mission/exercise portfolio’s that assist planners identifying the mission
requirement that drive logistics when applying supplies, equipment, material, and
personnel readiness.
2) Present and produce products consistent with the MCPP process.
3) Assist planners in identifying, assigning forces, and track tasks associated to the
execution of any mission. Once the unit is assigned the unit is responsible for dedicating
the appropriate personal and equipment within the work bench, upon association this
allows the unit and Higher headquarters a means to track the units’ readiness to
complete assigned mission.
4) Brief a commander from a mission/exercise dashboard within the program without
necessarily building briefing products outside the program.
5) Waterfall or make available and tie logistics into the operation thus, supporting a
common operating picture.
The desired end state provides a system for operations to plan. The system supports command
and control over where operations, logistics, and intelligence all intersect. When the basic staff
sections of operations, logistics, and intelligence all meet and touch in commonality, are
mutually supporting, and indirectly/directly influence the other; this is where command and
control exists and is exercised.

4.2 Doctrinal Shift in Defining a ‘Supply Chain’
Traditionally, we think of a supply chain in terms of goods or material. However, anything
tangible can be both in demand and be supplied. Even in this context, “inventory” has a
meaning of whether or not what is demanded is available or unavailable which is not necessarily
confined to materiel supplies or equipment. Therefore, within our military context we would
extend the meaning of the term “supply chain” to include information and services as well as
materiel as these are tangible products which can be forecast, accounted, inventoried, and
quantified.

4.3 Doctrinal Shift in Defining a ‘Request’
Our understanding of a request is placing a demand for something. Usually, we think of this
demand primarily in terms of materiel, occasionally services, and very rarely applying it to
information. However, agnostic of traditional views and focusing on the function of the request
itself the analysis proposes a request would apply to all three of the tangible areas of
information, service, and materiel regardless of logistics function or even warfighting function.

90

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004

Figure 24: Three Request Types

4.3.1 Request for Information
As a tangible product, information represents anything the customer, observer, or consumer
requires to accomplish their mission or conversely sends to another so they can accomplish
their mission. The Marine Corps has established information and information supply chains
deliberately but unknowingly.
For instance, a SALUTE report is a pre-formatted request for information on enemy activity.
Planners want to know around the battlespace where the enemy is active and if a target of
opportunity has presented itself. Additionally, such a report and the data elements can be
analyzed to provide a pattern of activity or reveal enemy intentions. Thus, planners are
demanding (requesting) any and all units provide information on such activities through the preformatted information form.

4.3.2 Request for Service
Service is the action of assisting or doing work for someone else. This type of request is an end
action that is desired or demanded requiring a skill or application of skill plus equipment to
accomplish the desired outcome. While the servicing organization or individual may require
materials or information to conduct the work, the requesting or demanding organization is not
necessarily responsible in knowing and/or requesting materials directly for a service action.
Types of actions commonly categorized as “service” is a transportation request (i.e., CASEVAC,
MEDEVAC, maintenance contact team, recovery, EOD UXO removal/render safe, route
reconnaissance, etc.).

4.3.3 Request for Materiel
The most traditional view of a request as it applies to a tangible asset. Materiel is the finished
supplies and equipment required to be employed for the mission and support people.

4.3.4 Push versus Pull
Push and pull request system refers to two different approaches to managing the flow of
information, service, and material in supply chain management.
4.3.4.1 Push Strategy
A push strategy is positioned to be proactive and provide what the customer needs before the
customer requests them. This strategy requires an understanding of consumption rates and
forecasts as well as accounting for delays due to time/space between the customer and point of
delivery.
4.3.4.2 Pull Strategy
A pull strategy reactively provides what the customer needs in accordance with the demand of
the customer. Using this strategy conserves “inventory” and allocates resources only when
demanded.

91

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
4.3.4.3 Application of Push/Pull Strategies
Technically, every supply chain strategy is a hybrid between push and pull. A supply chain, no
matter if is supplying information, service, or material is designed to flip between push and pull
somewhere in the process. In all cases information, service, and material can be pushed or
pulled anywhere along the supply chain.

4.4 Logistics Planning, Forecasting, and Estimation Tools Gap
A comprehensive review of the logistics information technology systems reveals a significant
lack of planning, forecasting, and estimation tools within these existing programs. Most of these
logistics applications stop data development at “information” by displaying or providing reports
for the user to interpret as to the next step or action. Few applications had onboard dashboards
displaying trends over a period of time however, even these dashboards did not lend
themselves to a decision-making process and left interpretation to the user.
Users are forced to create their own intermediary planning, forecasting, and estimation tools
which are more than likely a form of Excel with complex formulas. Results from these tools are
then inputted into the various logistics IT programs for execution however, further tracking and
updates remained in these intermediary tools requiring constant user interaction and sharing via
email.

4.5 Recommended Doctrinal Changes to Transportation
4.5.1 Functional Change from ‘Transportation’ to ‘Mobility’
During the breakout sessions, the function of transportation under the current doctrinal structure
is limited in its ability to incorporate capabilities and technologies in the wider scope of the
competition continuum. How transportation is structured within the scope of Marine Corps
doctrine is extremely narrow in its ability to apply and guide logisticians in emerging
technologies as well as applying the appropriate level of data collection.

Figure 25: Legacy Transportation Sub-functions

4.5.1.1 Mobility versus Transportation
The rationale for the differentiation between the terms “transportation” and “mobility” is in the
context of doctrinal and policy development. Mobility is defined as the potential for movement
and the ability to move something or someone from one place to another using one or more
modes of transport (intermodal) to meet the needs of the organization. Mobility focuses on
satisfactorily meeting demands, while transport (including mode, infrastructure, and
accessibility) is the instrument which is required to carry out or realize mobility. Due to spatial
separation of military activities, a demand for transport services arises. The type of transport
services chosen to meet this need for mobility is the result of the platforms on hand, availability,

92

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
and accessibility coupled with the decision process of capacity planning of the available modes
in relation to the threat/environment the requested action must take place.
4.5.1.2 Intermodal Nature of Transportation
Through the process development of the Logistics IT OAG, it was noted transportation of both
people and things are interdependent. When using more than one transportation mode in the
conveyance of people or things is the definition of “intermodal.” Therefore, the doctrinal position
of the Marine Corps is to group transportation methods together to communicate the modes
available but also within the context of interdependencies of each mode in its conveyance. The
deployment of Marines usually starts with vehicles, then to aircraft or ships, then to vehicles
which illustrates intermodal connections in this scenario.

4.5.2 Littoral Capability Gap
The adversary threat and operating environment has given rise to the importance of littoral
regions. By the way of definition, “littoral” refers to an area of or relating to a coastal or shore
region to include a lake, sea, ocean or river, or any body of water (natural or man-made) with a
shore region.
Under the littoral definition and competition continuum comes the requirement for littoral
connectors that are smaller than current inventory is providing. The current inventory is built for
major beach head operations (i.e., LCU or LCAC) that are owned by the Department of the
Navy and operated by Naval personnel. The requirement for smaller more agile vessels that
may be autonomous or operated by Marine Corps personnel and wholly owned by the Marine
Corps is becoming more of an operational reality. Therefore, this necessitates a doctrinal
change to account for both emerging technologies, current/future requirements, and anticipated
employment by the organization. Therefore, three operating capabilities make up the littoral
mode:
4.5.2.1 Surface
The term “surface” means a vessel or watercraft operating on the surface of the water, including
both manned and unmanned watercraft but is not capable of transitioning to a submersible or
semisubmersible state.
4.5.2.2 Submersible
The term “submersible” means a vessel or watercraft capable of operating completely below the
surface of the water, including both manned and unmanned watercraft. While such craft can, at
times, operate on the surface the central capability determines its classification.
4.5.2.3 Semisubmersible
The term “semi-submersible” means any watercraft or vessel constructed or adapted to be
capable of operating with most of its hull and bulk under the surface of the water, including both
manned and unmanned watercraft. Operating with most of the hull under the surface of the
water reduces the silhouette and cross-sectional profile from surveillance, detection, and
targeting.

4.5.3 Unmanned and Small Aerial Craft Capabilities Gap
Emerging technologies in Unmanned Aircraft Systems (UAS), Unmanned Aerial Vehicles
(UAV), and small aerial craft are becoming more prominent. As these technologies become
more common place what is becoming apparent is owning and operating aerial craft is no longer
the sole responsibility of the Marine Corps’ Air Component. Aerial craft have the ability to lift

93

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
small cargo packages, deliver ordnance, and transport people and are becoming more
integrated into transportation mode selection.
4.5.3.1 Unmanned Aircraft Systems (UAS)
UAS encompasses the entire system that supports and controls the UAV, including ground
control stations, software, and communication systems. These components are ultimately
necessary for the safe operation of the UAV within the operating environment. Such
components can be as simple as a radio controlling device to more elaborate components such
as satellite positioning, high frequency radio, and optical infrared guidance systems.
4.5.3.2 Unmanned Aerial Vehicles (UAV)
The UAV is what is commonly known as the “drone.” It is the actual inflight mechanism to
provide surveillance, information, ordnance, or delivery of material. UAVs range in size from as
small as a couple of inches with a range of just 100 yards to as large as small airplane with a
range of 100,000 kilometers.
4.5.3.3 Small Aerial Craft
Small aerial craft are manned, autonomous or piloted aircraft which are capable of carrying one
or two persons but are not as complex as traditional aircraft as they fly in a limited range and
lower altitudes. Since these small aerial aircraft operate slower and at lower altitudes that do not
require special training to operate, they do not rely on or require traditional air traffic control
methods and can achieve autonomous flight. Commonly known as “personal drones” or
“personal flying vehicles” these capabilities open up a new space for what was commonly
considered “flying cars.” Technological advances in this area have created a new mode of
transportation which is more accessible and capable for short or medium haul requirements
(i.e., MEDIVAC, CASEVAC, Contact Teams, emergency services, etc.) that are faster and
cheaper compared to mass or bulk ground transportation options and require less support than
traditional fixed/rotor wing assets.

4.5.4 Logical Sub-functional Sequence
From an execution perspective, the sub-functions follow a “build on” data flow. Each of the four
proposed sub-functions add to the successive data layer in the process of mobility as a whole to
accomplish the assigned mission.

Figure 26: Mobility Data Continuum

4.5.4.1 Embarkation
While we understand embarkation as the process of putting personnel and/or equipment into
ships, vehicles, and/or aircraft, this is not usually decided at the tactical unit unless those assets
are organically owned and operated. Embarkation at the tactical unit is the action of identifying
and planning for the people and things to be embarked. It is more akin for a request for service.
Using manning documents for people and equipment density lists for things, embarkation

94

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
personnel collect weights, measurements, and associated other data points to the next
sequence of capacity planning. From the tactical unit perspective, embarkation is the
preparatory work for the request for lift/movement by providing the requisite data.
4.5.4.2 Capacity Planning
At the higher-level capacity is matched with the provided embarkation data. Even in terms of the
tactical unit, capacity planning is occurring. Capacity planning is the process of determining the
demands of the mission with available and accessible assets. This action occurs at all levels
from the unit’s motor transport section to TRANSCOM, formally and informally, agnostic to scale
and scope regardless of transportation mode. Therefore, the elevation as a subfunction
adjacent to embarkation ensures deliberate planning is considered and builds upon the previous
embarkation request data.
4.5.4.3 Transportation Mode
The output of the capacity planning process is transportation mode selection. The assumption
should be made that the complete capacity plan will be intermodal by nature, meaning more
than one mode is in support of the overall mission demand. Within this subfunction are the
internal actions of assigning, allocating, dispatching, etc. The data drivers are common to all
levels in scale and scope as someone owns, operates, and directs the assets to support the
assigned mission.
4.5.4.4 Movement Execution
Movement execution becomes working action under the assigned plan. Tracking and monitoring
of the movement is a general requirement action which occurs at all levels. The end of the
action is the confirmation the people and assets arrived at the destination and the assigned lift
resources/transportation modes are returned. After action data is critical to the capacity planning
cycle for consumption, resource allocation, and time expended and must not be discarded.

4.5.5 Summary of Changes from Transportation to Mobility
The structural changes to this function of logistics elevates the enabling subfunctions and is
sequenced in a common occurring logical order.

95

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004

Figure 27: Proposed Mobility Sub-functions

The summary of changes corresponds to the numbers represented in the graphic.
1) Mobility. The ability, accessibility, and requirement to move people or things according to
the organizational goals and requirements.
2) People (PAX). Within embarkation the movement of people has drifted into the realm of
administration through the collection of manning or staffing documents. Calling out
people as a planning factor within embarkation drives the unification of planning back to
the embarkation function.
3) Things (Cargo). Within embarkation the things category is a separate category but on
par with people.
4) Capacity Planning. The next phase within the mobility function from embarkation data
collection to matching the need to move people and things to the available transportation
modes. Passenger transportation is focused on the moving people either individually or
as a group. Like passenger transportation, freight transportation is focused on the
material things.
5) Transportation Modes. Capacity planning allocates and aligns to the best transportation
modes accessible and available within the timeframe allocated. Within this subfunction,
each mode is agnostic if the assets are organic, commercial, chartered, or laterally
supported.
6) Littoral Mode. This mode, while a topic of greater conversation and is a wider influence
in the competition continuum, is not accounted for as a specific mode. Traditional view of
this mode is this is the Navy’s realm. However, with the need for smaller, versatile,
cheaper, and easily piloted systems the traditional view is being challenged. Waterborne
craft are becoming autonomous and in varying sizes indicating the Marine Corps will
own and operate these systems.
7) UAS/UAV & Small Aerial Craft. Typically, we see these as “drones” however, the
capability is widening as technologies mature. These systems will become more and
more common place transporting small and medium lift packages in and around the

96

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
battlespace as well as a low cost/low risk option in shuttling personnel between locations
without using traditional aircraft for administrative use. Technology allows the capability
to be employed without a large infrastructure and without extensive training.
8) Movement Execution. Establishing this subfunction encapsulates the action and decision
execution after the mode of transportation has been selected/assigned. Port/terminal
operations ensures the people/things are manifested, the transportation mode is
scheduled, and the throughput monitored for arrivals and departures. Landing Support
provides tactical support for tactical offload/onload of people/things without establishing
a permanent presence.

4.6 Recommended Doctrinal Changes to Supply
4.6.1 Redundant/Multiple Supply Chains
The analysis observed the redundant and multiple supply chains for each of the Class of
Supplies and in some cases for each sub-function. This is most pronounced and evident in
Class IX where the supply chain is divided between “consumable” and “secondary reparable”.
The secondary reparable parallel supply chain is a legacy process born out of cost reduction
and accountability from the late 1970’s. In order to reduce cost in the rebuild cycle, customers
are required to provide the necessary consumable parts (call a ‘configuration’) to the turn-in
facility along with non-working core (called a ‘carcass’). However, to ensure authorized issues
are made to authorized persons and the configurations are correct leads to an extensive
administrative burden to execute. For example:
1) Commanding Officers are required to generate letter correspondence for delayed turnins, issues without a corresponding turn-in, and turn-ins without a corresponding issue.
These administrative letters are required even though the same commanding officer
acting as an Approving Official has appointed representatives as a Financial Ordering
Official on the DD 577 form for the unit. This calls into question if the CO/AO has
assigned a financial representative on the DD 577 to obligate the command, why would
additional authorization/notification be required for the reparable exchange activity to
execute such issues/turn-ins? When faced with logistics administrative requirements, the
enterprise must call into question the process value and its relevance. Without
challenging these administrative requirements, the underlying processes become
needlessly redundant and create a slow/unresponsive/inefficient logistics apparatus.
2) The customer is required to travel to the reparable exchange activity to make an
exchange. Additionally, if the carcass doesn't meet 'configuration criteria,' it is rejected,
sending the customer back to correct it. The organization is expending man-hours and
material in the thousands to save a few hundred of dollars in easily obtained
consumable parts. The maintenance section expends significant resources in time and
distance to perform the SECREP exchange as it is a walk through, in person process.
Notably, these garrison administrative procedures were not followed in contingency operations.
At no time in OIF/OEF were commanders denied an issue because of a delayed turn-in letter.
Additionally, the SECREP exchange was not an in person, configuration check. SECREPs were
ordered as a Class IX consumable part and the carcass returned in the FASTPAC box the new
SECREP arrived.
The Marine Corps absolutely needs to collapse Class IX consumable and secondary reparable
supply chains into a single supply chain to remove the antiquated administrative requirements

97

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
and provide a more efficient and effective supply chain. In this concept the following changes
would apply:
1) All condition code “A” secondary reparable parts would fall under the intermediate supply
activity. All orders, backorders, fulfilment, and follow on shipping for secondary reparable
materiel is the responsibility of the intermediate supply activity.
2) The secondary reparable activity becomes the single supplier to the intermediate supply
activity.
3) Customers order secondary reparable parts at they would any Class IX. This includes
financially obligating the initial full price upon requisitioning.
4) The intermediate supply activity ships the secondary reparable to the customer. The
customer receives the notification of the refund limitations and core refund price list.
5) The customer is responsible for putting a clean and complete carcass with the shipping
paperwork back into the shipping box and is collected by the intermediate supply
delivery truck.
6) The delivery truck will bring all collected carcasses to the secondary reparable facility for
processing.
7) The secondary reparable facility will issue a financial refund for the core to the customer
based on the carcass condition:
a. Clean, complete, and paperwork is correct = 100% refund
b. Clean, incomplete, and paperwork is correct = 75% refund
c. Not clean, incomplete, and paperwork is correct = 65% refund
d. Not clean, incomplete, and paperwork is incorrect = 50% refund
e. No carcass returned after 30 days of new issue = 0% refund

4.6.2 Production/Manufacturing Capabilities Gap
The Marine Corps is transforming its supply chain to support internal production/manufacturing
of parts/products to overcome time and distance in the competition continuum as well as
supplement the peacetime challenges. Production/manufacturing is established in a “make or
buy” decision performed by the supply chain management apparatus through available data
(cost, time, location, transportability, etc.). The Marine Corps has yet to insert
production/manufacturing within the context of supply chain doctrine on the ground side.
The condition has been present and applicable with traditional subtractive manufacturing forms
and organic capabilities but has become most apparent with the rise and use of additive
manufacturing. Therefore, the Marine Corps has pervasive problems linking the supply chain
“make or buy” decision with existing and emerging production/manufacturing capabilities:
1) The requisite research and business case analysis to make or buy a part has not been
conducted to focus the available resources against critical parts for critical assets.
2) Production/manufacturing capabilities are not systematically available throughout the
supply chain either laterally or in a hierarchy nor are the capabilities centrally managed
and standardized.
3) The ability to select a part to be bought or made is not a deliberate supply chain decision
making process supported by data in cost, time, availability, and capability.

98

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
4) Logistics information systems are not enabled or capable to pass production requests
electronically to the production facility or to escalate to another production facility
(commercial or government).
Currently, the customer, usually the mechanic/technician, is required to be proactive in
searching for, activating, and making use of organic production/manufacturing capabilities to
satisfy demands. The current state ends up making the system reactionary, laborious,
administratively intensive, and creates data gaps essential to supply chain planning.
4.6.2.1 Make or Buy Decisions
Before a part or product is produced, a decision must be made based on the demand and what
is available. Decisions made regarding the external purchase or internal manufacture of a
product regardless of the method (traditional subtractive versus additive manufacturing) is a
supply chain management decision. This type of decision is known as a “make or buy” decision.
However, for this is supported by the analysis of data. To decide to make or buy requires a
make or buy analysis.
4.6.2.2 Make or Buy Analysis
The make or buy analysis is a process of gathering and organizing data about product
requirements, available supply chain data, and against available alternatives. This analysis then
determines the buy strategy and the make strategy for each part/product. Essential data
elements include:
1) Product buy information: cost to buy, lead time from order to receipt, product criticality,
end item information/criticality, average demand data over the course of three years, and
seasonality alpha adjustments.
2) Product make information: cost to make, make time (includes administration time and
post product finishing), available design, available material, make method,
meets/exceeds specification data, design package (for the combination of multiple
production methods i.e., plastic additive manufacturing and metal machine lathing to
produce an end product), and any approvals/conditional requirements.
The results of the make or buy analysis then informs the make or buy strategy.
4.6.2.3 Make or Buy Strategy
Make or buy strategy is applying the analysis to the overall impact and mission of the
organization as it pertains to the criticality of the part/product to the mission. The objective of the
make or buy strategy is to ensure the appropriate level of resources are applied to enable
part/product availability across the enterprise. The supply chain decision may not be traditionally
held at the organizational supply activity and the actual production/manufacturing also may not
be performed at the organizational supply activity either. However, the science and data
analysis are a supply function regardless of who or where it is performed.

99

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004

Figure 28: Make or Buy Strategy Matrix

The above graphic presents a make or buy strategy matrix example. The quadrant is indexed by
value/cost on the Y axis and the risk impact to mission on the X axis.
The four labeled quadrants represent the general impacts between cost/value and risk impacts
to the mission. An item best described by the quadrant then leads to the preferred make or buy
strategy for that item:
1) Routine (low cost/value; low risk impact). Item is low cost to buy and is not mission
critical if it is not available or becomes subject of a normal supply chain disruption.
Therefore, the strategy for this item is to buy and stock.
2) Leverage (high cost/value; low risk impact). High-cost items which are not typically
mission critical items. Therefore, the strategy for this item is to buy only when demanded
from a customer without stocking the item.
3) Bottleneck (low cost/value; high risk impact). This item is not a cost driver but has a
direct impact on the mission. Additionally, due to the limited number of suppliers or an
increased susceptibility to supply chain disruptions it is best to be prepared to make this
item when the traditional supply chain starts issuing backorders.
4) Critical (high cost/value; high risk impact). High dollar value coupled with the mission
critical nature of this part means operations will come to halt if not available. Therefore,
this item should be made to put on the shelf even if demands are infrequent.
4.6.2.4 Advanced Manufacturing as a Production/Manufacturing Enabler
Advanced manufacturing describes the use of technologies and techniques to enhance
production quality, efficiency, and process of manufacturing. Advanced Manufacturing
encompasses the following: [15]
1) Efficient Production. The technologies adopted are related to design, followed by
simulation. It addresses physical and computer modeling, all advanced production
technologies, and related control techniques that a manufacturing unit may need to
adopt. The focus is on simultaneous engineering rather than sequence based.
Production technologies include rapid prototype creation and near net shape
manufacturing as well as the techniques of precision casting, machining, and joining.

100

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
2) Intelligent Production. Intelligent production utilizes information communication
technology across manufacturing and logistics systems. This system focuses on creating
and implementing systems to optimize manufacturing facilities with effective monitoring
coupled with regularly updated maintenance and repair plans (i.e., linked to predictive
maintenance analysis or notifications).
3) Effective Organization. Effective organization relates to the development of effective
creation and coordination of manufacturing resources. It comprises material resources
as well as related knowledge and works in areas where technology can be used to
enhance the participation and abilities of a SME or a lateral organization. Effective
organization looks into related aspects of manufacturing such as sharing of facilities and
resources, incubation units for newer ideas and technologies, knowledge management,
and all aspects of product availability effectively becoming a cross-functional, multidiscipline organization.
4) Additive Manufacturing. Manufacturers use methods like 3-D printing, fused deposition
modeling, or powder-bed laser printing systems to create complex models or
replacement parts from a single material. Industries that use additive manufacturing are
the aerospace sector, the medical field, prototyping, automotive, and consumer goods
among many others. These technologies continue to cost less to implement.
5) Advanced and Composite Materials. Advanced materials enable extremely precise
composite blends for particular applications like blends of metals, plastics, glass, and
ceramics. Materials that require precision in its chemical and physical properties are
manipulated to help enable performance breakthroughs while reducing material tradeoffs. Composite materials used often include recyclable plastics, high-strength alloys,
and advanced ceramics, among others.
6) Robotics and Automation. Robotics fit in naturally among advanced manufacturing
techniques and are designed to automate processes that involve precision movements,
lifting heavy objects, and joining of elements--consistent across production units.
Additionally, robotics reduces the risk involved in hazardous jobs like the automotive and
aerospace sectors.
7) Laser Machining and Welding. The laser process enables precision in machining and
welding. Laser technology processes high precision parts rapidly as the integrity of the
component is maintained with lower and steady amounts of heat. Such processes are
used to make pressure vessels and proximity sensor welding. It is also seen in battery
welding and sensitive electronics.
8) Nanotechnology. Devices are becoming smaller, and naturally, the need for smaller
parts and components is on the rise. Nanotechnology has increasingly gained traction
among designers who are looking to increase functionality and decrease the size of the
devices. Nanotechnology has helped advanced manufacturing systems minimize their
overall footprint while maximizing functionality across production facilities.
9) Network and IT Integration. With the internet, connectivity is unparalleled, and
advancements in internet technology have enabled connectivity to and from systems
and machines as well. Communication between sectors can be electronic and need not
be done manually. With internet access to all aspects of manufacturing, there is an
instant notification on any issues that may arise, enabling preemptive repairs and saving
time and cost.

101

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
4.6.2.5 Advanced Manufacturing Implementation
Advanced manufacturing technologies are not plug-and-play and are dependent on skilled
people and formal processes. The Marine Corps needs to take four key steps for the
implementation of advanced manufacturing technologies: [15]
1) Syncing Manufacturing Strategy to Mission Strategy. The Marine Corps must have a well
thought out manufacturing strategy that ties in strongly with its mission/employment
strategy. This can be achieved by quantifying aspects such as lead times, quality of the
product, the flexibility of manufacturing, and costs. Once quantified, the data from these
two strategies can be used to create manufacturing goals. In turn, data insights will help
with capital expenditure and investment decisions for the adoption of newer technologies
and their implementation. These strategies can be difficult if leadership does not have
access to the right kind of data and the various cost options to achieve
mission/employment goals. Another issue is the disconnect between departments and
their strategies. The first issue can be handled by investing in strong data acquisition
and analytical capabilities.
2) Tapping Networks for Technology Identification. The Marine Corps needs a central core
production/manufacturing business development department to build strong internal and
external production/manufacturing networks to identify new technologies relevant to the
mission strategy as well as to build the key performance indicators (KPIs) to monitor
performance. The production/manufacturing network needs the right framework with
clear goals. Network performance must regularly be measured against a set of
quantified input and output metrics to establish overall effectiveness, mission impacts,
and value.
3) Technology Prioritization and Evaluation. Establish a strong set of processes in place to
quantify the cost and merits of each production/manufacturing technology option when
compared to the mission strategy. This process should be the central core
production/manufacturing business development department who are staffed by risk
takers and can pilot innovations in the right direction. Additionally, the department must
also be ready to pull the plug on failing technologies when needed. The department
needs the support of specialized analysts who can provide the right data and can
forecast the impact of technology across a transition period.
4) Rapid Yet Strategic Implementation of Technologies. The Marine Corps is having the
challenge of rapid piloting and rolling out of new technologies because it remains
apprehensive about implementing new technologies and tend to hold out until it is too
late to be effective or becomes immediately obsolete. It is difficult to balance the
everyday functioning of the enterprise while doing R&D with new technologies. The way
to handle this is to create a small, specialized team of uniformed “makers” paired with
and supported by civilian contractors that can independently work on the R&D aspect of
new technologies. This way, bringing in advanced technologies into the enterprise is
easier and operationalized for immediate use.
4.6.2.6 Challenges to Implementing Advanced Manufacturing
There are two main challenges to adopting advanced manufacturing: human resources and
technology. [15]
1) Human Resource Issues. There is an increasingly unmet demand for professionals with
IT and design skills at the management level who are risk takers to try something new.

102

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
There is a need for “makers”15 to possess advanced design skills for shorter, customized
production runs. Essential but not always available, is IT literacy and skill sets such as
an understanding of CAD/CAM and CNC machine-based software.
2) Investment Issues. Advanced manufacturing technologies naturally require large
investments in research and development. There is also a relatively high-risk factor
enterprises take on, which at times affects decision-making, ultimately innovation. In
several cases, organizations may find it difficult to access specific materials, or find it
challenging to understand the characteristics of the material. Evaluation techniques and
the processing of designs can also be complex to create and apply. Often simulation
capability adopted by an organization does not keep up with technological development,
causing delays. More cybersecurity investments are needed to keep up with technology
innovations.
4.6.2.7 Additive Manufacturing
Additive manufacturing is a subset of Advanced Manufacturing and is of particular interest to the
Marine Corps because of the flexibility it provides relative to its physically small footprint making
it ideal for deploying this capability forward.
By definition, additive manufacturing is the computer-controlled process of building a 3D object
by compiling layer upon layer of material, typically ceramic or metal powder, on a build platform
until the final product is finished. The layers are hardened using heat, a curing agent, or lasers.
Since this method is additive, there’s less waste than with subtractive manufacturing, and
therefore, lower costs. [16]
4.6.2.8 Eight Types or Methods of Additive Manufacturing
The following is currently the types/methods of additive manufacturing each having unique
differences. [16]
1) Binder Jetting. Binder jetting, also known as material jetting or inkjet powder printing, is
among the most common additive manufacturing types. This method works similarly to
an office printer, except it prints three-dimensional objects. Instead of jetting ink onto a
page, binder jetting jets adhesive into a powder material. The print head moves
horizontally and vertically, putting down a new layer of build material with every pass.
This method uses a variety of materials: gypsum powder (chalk), plastics (usually
polypropylene), ceramics, metal (usually alloys), glass, and sand. Binder jetting is an
affordable additive manufacturing process due to its relatively low cost of entry and the
inexpensive nature of its materials. It can also create objects faster than most other
additive manufacturing processes in full color. Although it comes with many benefits,
binder jetting products tend to be fragile and require post-processing, including cleaning
and hardening, which can be tedious and time-consuming.
2) Directed Energy Deposition (DED). Directed energy deposition (DED) uses welding
principles to create three-dimensional objects. The material - typically metal wire or
powder - is melted by a focused energy source (laser or electron beam). The liquid
material is then precisely poured onto the build platform, where it quickly hardens,
forming a layer. This process repeats until the object is finished printing. One of DED’s
significant benefits is that it can be used for more than just item creation; it can also

15 “Maker” in this sense means someone who creates or invents things, either using traditional

crafts/methods or technology or the combination of both

103

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
repair and add material to existing parts or preforms. Additionally, it can utilize multiple
materials in a single printing process.
3) Material Extrusion. Material extrusion works similarly to a hot glue gun. The material
feeds into the printer from a coil. The tip of the nozzle heats and melts the material. The
liquid material is then placed layer by layer on the build platform, where it can cool and
solidify, forming the object. While this method is the most inexpensive method of additive
manufacturing, material extrusion isn’t without its limitations. Since the heating elements
aren’t powerful enough to melt high-density materials like metal, it is used primarily for
plastic polymers - which may not be durable enough for some applications, such as
tooling and fixturing.
4) Powder Bed Fusion (PBF). Powder bed fusion, otherwise known as electron beam
melting (EBM), starts with a large bed of powdered material, typically plastic, metal,
sand, or ceramic powders mixed with sand. The powder is selectively fused together
using a laser or electron beam. Once a layer of material is fused, the working area
moves down, and the new layer is built on top using the same process. With PBF,
there’s a high complexity level to the objects, making them stronger than objects created
by some other types of additive manufacturing. Since it requires a bed of powder, it is
difficult to maintain a clean working environment as it produces airborne dust. This
method is not the best option for tight spaces which do not have dust abatement.
5) Sheet Lamination. Sheet lamination, otherwise known as ultrasonic additive
manufacturing (UAM) or laminated object manufacturing (LOM) - is an additive
manufacturing process that stacks thin sheets of material and bonds them together
through ultrasonic welding, bonding, or brazing. As the layers stack up, the object takes
shape. Once all the layers are stacked and laminated, a CNC machine or laser cutter
removes excess material, creating the object’s final form. Since it works in multiple
layers, sheet lamination allows manufacturers to layer various materials, print in full
color, and at a much lower cost than some other additive manufacturing processes.
While sheet lamination is relatively inexpensive, it does require some post-processing,
which can be tedious and time-consuming. This process is limited to materials that can
come in sheets, such as paper, plastic, and metal. Since objects need to be trimmed
once lamination is complete, more waste is involved in this additive manufacturing
process compared to others.
6) Vat Polymerization. Vat polymerization is similar to powder bed fusion, except instead of
a bed of powder, it uses a vat of photopolymer resin, which is hardened in layers by an
ultraviolet laser. Once a layer is complete, more resin is added, and the next layer gets
built. This process continues until the object is complete. Although resin can be
expensive, vat polymerization can construct objects quickly while providing a high level
of accuracy and a good finish. Despite its benefits, vat polymerization manufacturers are
limited to using photo-resin materials and are required to perform a substantial level of
post-processing. The printed objects need to be removed from the resin and cleaned
before use.
7) Material Jetting. Similar to binder jetting, material jetting layers material to construct an
object. However, instead of layering adhesive on a bed of powder, material jetting melts
wax-like materials and precisely deposits droplets onto the build platform. As the layers
build up, the object takes shape. Many manufacturers utilize material jetting because it’s
relatively inexpensive and provides outstanding accuracy with high-quality surface
finishes. Unfortunately, material jetting is limited to using only wax-like materials, which
can be fragile as well as a longer build time.

104

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
8) Cold Spray. Cold spray additive manufacturing (CSAM) (also called cold spray 3D
printing) is a particular application of cold spraying, able to fabricate freestanding parts
or to build features on existing parts or components. During the process, fine powder
particles are accelerated in a high velocity compressed gas stream, and upon the impact
on a substrate or backing plate, deform and bond together creating a layer. Moving the
nozzle over a substrate repeatedly, a deposit is building up layer-by-layer, to form a part
or component. A robot or computer-controlled manipulator controls the spray gun
movements which complex shapes can be created. To achieve 3D shape, there are two
different approaches. First, fix the substrate and move the cold spray gun/nozzle on a
robotic arm or second, move the substrate with a robotic arm and keep the spray-gun
nozzle fixed. There is also a possibility to combine these two approaches either using
two robotic arms or other manipulators. The process always requires a substrate and
uses only powder as raw material. This technique is distinct from selective laser melting
or electron-beam additive manufacturing or other additive manufacturing process using
laser or electron beam for melting the feedstock materials.
4.6.2.9 Emerging Technologies
Advance manufacturing is rapidly driving major performance improvements in the industry
sector. Newer model machines are allowing for unique and obscure product development with
less waste and more stability. Additionally, emerging technologies are becoming faster and
more accurate stream-lining the journey from product decision to product making to product
delivery. Because AM depends on optimizing problems many users approach concepts with a
"best guess" scenario, design the product, calculate the failure, redesign, produce and then
redesign which is time consuming and at best suboptimal for limited resource AM
manufacturing. Artificial intelligence (AI) is one emerging technology that takes the guess work
out of calculations and adjusts to the design to ensure the best product is manufactured.
1) Contour Crafting. Uses a computer-controlled crane or gantry to build large structures
fast using quick-setting concrete "like" material. With this technology, NASA is starting to
refine the process to build on the moon and eventually Mars. Contour crafting machines
have been developed and are being tested to utilize other materials such as
thermoplastics, thermosets, and various ceramics.
2) Generative Design. Digital simulation and analysis can be evaluated in seconds.
Algorithms can automatically adjust the geometry of a part between simulations, with no
manual refinement required. Using artificial-intelligence techniques, these new
generative design systems can compare the results of thousands of simulations to close
in on a design that delivers the most favorable combination of attributes,
3) Ultrasonic Additive Manufacturing (UAM). A technique which employs high-frequency
vibrations to fuse thin sheets of metal together without alterations to the microstructure
of the metal. Currently in use by NASA.
4) Meta Material. A synthetic composite material with structures that are not found in
natural materials.
4.6.2.10 Required Data Capture Points
Through the LOG IT OAG, it was apparent the Marine Corps was not capturing key data points
which would enable the make or buy analysis and decisions. Additionally, existing data is not
curated to provide the necessary insights in the use, cost, and criticality of each part in context
of the item to which it belongs or is used. The following key data points are required for capture:

105

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
1) Item Reference. The original manufactured part reference number (i.e., NSN). This is not
the production version item reference which could be different.
2) Production Flag. The flag indicates the item can currently be produced (Y or N).
3) Production Mode. Production mode this applies (i.e., subtractive manufacturing, additive
manufacturing, hybrid, etc.)
4) Production Cost. The total average cost, to include waste, to produce the item.
5) Production Time. The total average time, to include finishing, to produce the item.
6) Design Approved. The approved design is on file and is currently in an approved status
(Y or N). Approved designs generally indicates the design and identified material meets
or exceeds the original item specifications in fit, function, and performance.
7) Quality Control Flag. If the item requires a formal quality control inspection
postproduction (Y or N).
8) Producer(s). Listing of producers who are capable of providing the finished product and
serves as the point of shipment.
4.6.2.11 Production/Manufacturing Summary
Organic and emerging production/manufacturing capabilities are part of the “make or buy”
supply chain management decision process. However, these capabilities are not currently
nested into the doctrinal function of supply and have been essentially put into the “maintenance”
function.
Additionally, the Marine Corps is not poised to formally align these functional capabilities
deliberately into the supply chain as it does collect/possess the data necessary to support the
make or buy analysis for each potential part it could/should make and does not have the
logistics IT solutions enabled to capitalize on integrating, tracking, and costing requests for
produced part/products.

4.6.3 Distribution
Through the analysis, there seems to be an over emphasis of the function of distribution as a
function of transportation to the point it seems these two functions have essentially merged.
However, this should not be the case as at the functional level.
The Joint Publication 4-09, Distribution Operations, 05 February 2010 defines the aspect of
distribution: “It is the operational process of synchronizing all elements of the logistic system to
deliver the “right things” to the “right place” at the “right time” to support the geographic
combatant commander (GCC).”
Additionally, the Marine Corps echoes distribution as, “an official delivery of anything, such as
orders or supplies and that functional phase of military logistics that embraces the act of
dispensing materiel, facilities, and services.”
The Marine Corps should refocus its efforts away from emphasizing distribution as a function of
mobility. Functionally, distribution is bringing demand and capacity together in order to make
supportability/sustainment decisions, which includes transportation modes but is not exclusively
mutual.

106

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004

4.6.4 Digital Tracking of Military Equipment
Through the analysis of determining asset/equipment data reliability, the enterprise does not
possess “digital truth” of military equipment and assets anywhere at any time. There are two
basic questions the enterprise struggles with:
1) Do I have all the equipment assigned to me in my possession (does it exist)?
2) Where is this equipment physically located (where is it or when was it last “seen”)?
To the first question, many assume they have the equipment in their possession and are
required to check quarterly, annually, and/or as occurring by reconciling the digital report and
manually matching physical assets. Currently, this method expends hundreds of man-hours
reconciling the physical world with the digital world and usually, with limited success.
Additionally, the report is a snapshot in time and only addresses the first question and not the
second question.
To the second question, many correlate physicality of the equipment to the DoDAAC. For
instance, if the equipment is owned by 3d Maintenance Battalion and the TAC 2 address is
"Camp Kinser, Okinawa" it is assumed, for the most part, the equipment is physically on
Okinawa and on Camp Kinser. However, Camp Kinser, Okinawa is the owner's address not the
physical location of the equipment. 3d Maintenance Battalion has its equipment spread from
Camp Kinser to Camp Hansen and supports contact teams in Korea, Philippines, Guam, and
Hawaii.
To verify both existence and physical location takes a human being to go out to the lots, bays,
rooms, warehouses, etc. to physically check and verify. If the equipment is not where it is
supposed to be or thought to be leads to a process of research to determine when and where
the equipment was last seen and its current location. Therefore, the enterprise as a whole does
not possess “digital truth” of its equipment and assets as it fully relies on to ask "someone" (a
human being) to go out and physically verify that X item with Y serial number is in location Z and
if not, to research to figure disparities and discrepancies.
Due to the analog nature and lag between digital reporting and physical reality, it is impossible
to fully perform force level equipment sourcing and formulate equipment density list on hands.

exists.

4.7 Health Services Capability Gap
Pertaining to the INDOPACOM operational scenario, the medical planners are forecasting
requirements based on the assumption units will need an initial 15-day medical supply, after
which will require some kind of resupply. This operating concept—as envisioned—will have
small teams operating out of austere outposts over vast geographic distances with extremely
limited reach back support. That scenario demands our forces be as self-sufficient as possible,
necessitating that Marines and Corpsman leverage resources available in their locales and
foraging for various classes of supply.
One of the common After-Action Report (AARs) narratives from the Marine Special Operations
Companies (MSOC)/Marine Special Operations Teams (MSOT) returning from deployment to
the Philippines was, “have more IV bags on-hand”, at least initially. Basilan, Philippines is hot
and humid, and during sustained operating conditions, people dehydrate very quickly—
especially when they’re not acclimated properly. If the scenario for which we are preparing ever
manifests, the first medical supply to be depleted will be saline intravenous fluid (IV) bags, as is
always the case. Once those get used up, then what? A single IV bag weighs 2 pounds and is
sensitive to the elements. Additionally, it cannot be assumed the local populace and/or location
will have enough IV bags—or any medical resources—to share with the miliary, especially given
the contested transportation and supply chains. This situation poses a very real, tangible risk to

109

Logistics Data Exchange Requirements at the Tactical Edge
FA8075-18-D-0004
human life. That risk is also unnecessary, as we have the capacity to avoid this scenario before
crisis emerges.
To avoid the logistically-contested aspect of medical supply availability, movement, storage, and
tracking, the capability to produce things like saline IV bags locally and on-demand is essential.
This capability has two developments:
1) NASA made a saline IV bag machine for the International Space Station (ISS)—arguably
the most austere environment known to man. Therefore, NASA is not paying money and
sacrificing limited space and weight on already-constrained rockets to transport
intravenous fluids into orbit.
2) The Army has begun research and has gone through some testing for a similar
capability: https://www.thedefensepost.com/2021/01/29/device-iv-fluid-woundedsoldiers/
Sustainment of forces requires the development and procurement of self-generating systems to
provide resuscitation fluids like 0.9 percent saline, and an additional requirement to increase
that concentration to 3% for Damage Control Resuscitation Treatments (DCRT) in treatment of
Traumatic Brain Injuries (TBI).

FA8075-18-D-0004
